Capability for Faith is a Divine Grace
Ash'ari argues that the ability to believe is a special grace that implies divine guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that the capability for faith is a divine grace that specifically belongs to believers, not disbelievers.

Core Idea
Capability for faith is not neutral – it is a grace that would produce faith if possessed.

Imam Ash'ari presents a logical argument to show that the ability to believe is not a neutral capacity but a special gift from Allah that inherently leads to belief. He challenges his opponents to accept that if disbelievers truly had this capability, they would be guided and praiseworthy – which they clearly are not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice how Ash'ari uses questions to lead the opponent.
2
Identify the logical moves
Break down the argument: the initial question about capability being a grace, the opponent's concession, the consequence that it must be tawfiq/tasdid, and the application to disbelievers.
The key is the conditional: if disbelievers had the capability, they would be guided and praiseworthy.
3
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on why Ash'ari concludes that capability for faith is exclusive to believers. The argument shows that the capability itself, being a grace, would produce faith, so disbelievers cannot have it.
This guards against the misconception that capability is neutral.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۰۸ - مسألة في الاستطاعة : ويقال لهم : أليست استطاعة الإيمان نعمة من الله عز وجل وفضلاً وإحساناً؟ ذلك . فإذا قالوا: نعم . قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون توفيقاً وتسديداً، فلا بد من الإجابة إلى ذلك . يقال لهم : فإذا كان الكافرون قادرين على الإيمان، فما أنكرتم أن يكونوا موفقين للإيمان، ولو كانوا موفقين مسددين لكانوا ممدوحين، وإذا لم يجز ذلك لم يجز أن يكونوا على الإيمان قادرين، ووجب أن يكون الله عز وجل اختص بالقدرة على الإيمان المؤمنين .
208 – Issue concerning capability (istiṭāʿa): And it is said to them: Is not the capability for faith a grace from God Almighty, a favor, and a beneficence? That. If they say: Yes. It is said to them: So what prevents you from [affirming] that it is success (tawfīq) and guidance (tasdīd)? There is no avoiding answering that. It is said to them: If the disbelievers are capable of faith, then what prevents you from [affirming] that they are successful (muwaffaqūn) for faith? And if they were successful and guided, they would be praiseworthy. And since that is not permissible, it is not permissible that they are capable of faith, and it is necessary that God Almighty singled out the believers with the capability for faith.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) that the capability (istiṭāʿa) for faith is a divine grace that entails guidance (tawfīq and tasdīd). Ash'ari begins by asking the opponent: Is the ability to believe a grace and favor from God? If they affirm (as they must), he then asks: Why do you deny that this ability is therefore a form of divine success (tawfīq) and guidance (tasdīd)? The opponent must answer. Then Ash'ari turns to the case of disbelievers: If disbelievers are truly capable of faith (as the opponent claims), then they would also be successful and guided by that same ability. But if they were successful and guided, they would be praiseworthy. However, it is not permissible (lā yajūz) to consider disbelievers praiseworthy, so it is not permissible that they are capable of faith. Therefore, it is necessary that God exclusively gives the capability for faith to believers. The argument is logical: capability for faith, being a grace, would necessarily result in being guided and thus praiseworthy; since disbelievers are not guided nor praiseworthy, they cannot possess that capability.

Key Terms
استطاعة (capability)
توفيق (success)
تسديد (guidance)
ممدوح (praiseworthy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That capability for faith is a neutral capacity that does not inherently lead to faith.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that the capability is a divine grace that, if possessed, would necessarily produce faith and make the person praiseworthy. Hence it cannot be neutral.

Checkpoint
Why does Ash'ari argue that disbelievers cannot have the capability for faith?
A) Because they lack the physical ability to believe.
B) Because if they had it, they would be guided and praiseworthy, which they are not.
C) Because God predestines them to disbelief without any capability.
D) Because capability for faith is granted only after faith is chosen.

Key Takeaway
The capability for faith is a divine grace that, if possessed, would necessarily lead to faith and praise; therefore, it belongs only to believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that your own effort or choice was the primary cause of your faith? How does this passage’s assertion that capability for faith is a pure grace, not a neutral capacity, challenge that assumption—and what must you do in response right now?
?
If the capability for faith is a divine grace that only Allah grants, and you are among the believers, does that not demand from you a deeper gratitude and humility? In what specific way will you express that today, not just in feeling but in action?
?
Consider a non-believer you know. This passage implies they lack the very capability for faith—a grace they never received. How does that reality change your attitude toward them? Are you compelled to despair or to pray? What concrete step will you take to seek Allah's mercy for them?

Distinguishing Capability for Faith from Disbelief
Ash'ari proves through supplication that capability for faith differs from capability for disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses the practice of supplication (raghba) to prove that the power (qudra) for disbelief is not the same as the power for faith.

Core Idea
Believers only pray for the power to believe – proving the two powers are distinct.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a crisp logical argument to refute the claim that the same capability serves both faith and disbelief. He points to an observable fact: believers consistently ask God for the power to believe, not for the power to disbelieve. If both were identical, such prayers would make no sense. This brief but powerful reasoning establishes a key distinction in the discussion of divine capacity (qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then' and the observed fact.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The passage begins with a hypothetical: 'If the power for disbelief were the power for faith...' Understand what this implies.
The consequence is that one would pray for the power to disbelieve.
3
Observe the real-world evidence
Ash'ari then states what we actually see: believers pray for the power to believe, not for disbelief. This is the key observational evidence.
This observation contradicts the expected consequence of the hypothetical.
4
Grasp the conclusion
From the contradiction, the original premise must be false. Therefore, the power for disbelief is not the same as the power for faith.
This conclusion has important implications for understanding divine decree and human capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۰۹- مسألة أخرى : ويقال لهم : لو كانت القدرة على الكفر قدرة على الإيمان فقد رغب إليه في القدرة على الكفر ، فلما رأينا المؤمنين يرغبون إلى الله عز وجل في قدرة الإيمان،
209 – Another issue: And it is said to them: If the power (qudra) for disbelief were the power for faith, then one would have supplicated to Him for the power for disbelief. But we see the believers supplicating to God Almighty for the power for faith.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a short but pointed argument (istidlāl) distinguishing the power (qudra) to disbelieve from the power to believe. Ash'ari presents a conditional: If the power to disbelieve were the same power as to believe, then one could legitimately pray to God for the power to disbelieve. However, we observe in reality that believers only pray for the power to believe, not for the power to disbelieve. This observable practice shows that the two powers are distinct. The argument relies on the fact that supplication (raghba) is directed towards obtaining what is good, and believers do not ask for the power to sin. Hence, the power to disbelieve cannot be identical to the power to believe. The conclusion implied is that God grants believers a specific capability for faith that is not given to disbelievers.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
كفر (disbelief)
إيمان (faith)
رغب (supplicate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the same power (qudra) serves both good and evil actions.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that if that were true, one could legitimately pray for power to disbelieve, but believers never do; thus the powers are separate.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Ash'ari offer to show that the power for disbelief differs from the power for faith?
A) Logical impossibility of identical opposites.
B) Believers only pray for the power for faith, not for disbelief.
C) Quranic verses explicitly separate the two powers.
D) Disbelievers never have any power.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari demonstrates through the practice of supplication that the capability for faith is distinct from the capability for disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever ask for the power to sin or disbelieve? Why? What does your prayer list reveal about your true desires?
?
If the power for faith is a distinct gift from God, not something you can generate yourself, how does that change the way you thank Him for your faith? What will you thank Him for today?
?
The believers' practice of supplicating only for faith is presented as a proof. Examine your own heart: do you secretly desire the power to sin without consequences? How does this passage expose that? What will you change about your supplication habits starting today?

Divine Decree
beginner
5 min
God's Special Grace for Believers
Refutes an objection about God's creation of limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that God's creation of limbs determines faith, and why God's exclusive blessings for believers are just.

Core Idea
Having limbs does not prevent disbelief; God's special grace does.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari tackles a common objection against divine justice: if God guides some but not others, is that unfair? He uses a simple refutation involving limbs to show that material creation does not determine faith. Let's follow his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the rhetorical question that sets up the refutation.
2
Identify the objection
The passage refutes an unstated objection: that God's creation of limbs for believers prevents them from following Satan, implying that if God gave everyone limbs, all would be believers. Find how this is presented.
3
Understand the rhetorical question
The author asks: 'Is it permissible to say to believers: Had I not created hands and feet for you, you would have followed Satan?' Why is this question effective?
It exposes the flaw: disbelievers also have limbs yet follow Satan.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that Allah singles out believers with special blessings and success. Reflect on why this refutes the objection.
The key concept is 'ikhtisas' — special selection.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. It begins by stating that disbelievers are followers of Satan and will be in the Fire. Then it poses a rhetorical question: is it permissible to say to believers that if God had not created hands and feet for them, they would have followed Satan? The same hands and feet were given to disbelievers, yet they followed Satan. So if the opponent denies that such a statement is permissible, then their own claim (that God's giving success to believers alone is unjust) is similarly impermissible. The passage concludes that God singles out believers with special blessings and success not given to disbelievers, thereby establishing God's favor and justice.

Capability for Faith: A Divine Gift for Believers Only
Ash'ari proves that capability for faith is exclusive to believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that capability for faith is a divine gift that God gives exclusively to believers, and why disbelievers cannot be capable of faith.

Core Idea
Capability for faith is not inherent—it is a blessing God grants only to believers.

This lesson examines a dialectical proof from Imam al-Ash'ari regarding the nature of capability (istita'a) for faith. The argument proceeds by securing a concession from the opponent—that capability is a divine blessing—and then drawing out the necessary conclusion: that disbelievers cannot possess such capability. This reasoning establishes that faith is not a matter of human effort alone but a gift of divine grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the English translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the key steps of the argument
The argument moves from a concession to an inference to a conclusion. Map out the logic: question, concession, inference, reduction, conclusion.
Pay attention to the conditional reasoning.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this matters for understanding predestination (qadar) and God's role in granting faith. How does this shape our view of human effort?
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology.

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) concerning the nature of capability (istita'a) for faith. It begins by asking whether the capability for faith is a blessing from God. The opponent agrees. Then it argues that this capability is identical to success (tawfiq) and guidance (tasdid). It then reasons: if disbelievers are capable of faith, they would have been given success and would thus be praiseworthy. But they are not praiseworthy, so they cannot be capable. Therefore, God has singled out believers with the capability for faith.

The Distinction Between Power for Faith and Power for Disbelief
Ash'ari proves the distinction between two powers via believers' actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why believers' supplications demonstrate that the power for faith and the power for disbelief are distinct capacities.

Core Idea
Believers' prayers reveal that faith-power and disbelief-power are different.

In this brief but powerful argument, Imam al-Ash'ari draws on a simple observation of Muslim practice. Believers consistently ask Allah for the power to believe and avoid the power to disbelieve. If both powers were the same, why would they distinguish in their supplications? The passage uses this to refute the idea that a single power can produce both faith and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the logic: the opponent claims the powers are identical; the author presents evidence from believers' behavior.
The Arabic is concise.
2
Understand the conditional argument
The author uses a conditional: if power for disbelief equals power for faith, then one would desire the power for disbelief. But believers desire faith-power and renounce disbelief-power. So they are different.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for the doctrine of qadar. Distinguishing the powers helps clarify that Allah gives different capacities for different actions.
This ties to the broader debate on human agency.

Source Text

Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an argument distinguishing between the power (qudra) for faith and the power for disbelief. The opponent claims they are the same. The author observes that believers pray to God for the power to believe and shun the power to disbelieve. If both powers were identical, believers would not distinguish in their supplications. Therefore, the two powers are distinct.

Divine Favor and Command: The Optional Nature of Grace
Defines favor as optional and applies to God's command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari concept of divine favor as optional, and how this applies to God's command to believe.

Core Idea
Divine favor is a gift God may give or withhold; it is not owed.

Imam al-Ash'ari here tackles a key question about faith: is the strength to believe something God must grant, or a favor He may give? The answer distinguishes between favor and entitlement, and helps explain how God can command faith even from those He does not empower.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of question and answer.
Notice the rhetorical structure: question, then definition, then application.
2
Define key terms
Identify 'fadl' (favor) and 'tafaddul' (granting favor) and contrast with 'istihqaq' (entitlement).
The difference is that favor is optional, entitlement is obligatory.
3
Apply the concept
Consider the implication that God may command faith while withholding the power – this is the Ash'ari position.
This shows that command does not imply empowerment.

210 - مسألة أخرى: ويقال لهم : أخبرونا عن قوة الإيمان أليست فضلاً من الله عز وجل؟ فلا بد من نعم. فيقال لهم : فالتفضل أليس هو ما للمتفضل أن لا يتفضل به، وله أن يتفضل به؟ فلا بد من الإجابة إلى ذلك بنعم ؛ لأن ذلك هو الفرق بين الفضل وبين الاستحقاق ويقال لهم : وللمتفضل إذا أمر بالإيمان أن يرفع التفضل ولا يتفضل به فيأمرهم بالإيمان، وإن خذلهم لم يعطهم قدرة الإيمان، وهذا هو قولنا ومذهبنا.

And it is said to them: The bestower, when He commands faith, may remove the favor and not bestow it, while commanding them to faith, and if He abandons them (khadhalahum) He does not give them the power for faith. Commentary
This passage is a definition and application of the concept of favor (fadl). It asks whether the strength of faith is a favor from God. The answer is yes. Then it explains that a favor is something the giver can choose to give or withhold, as opposed to entitlement (istihqaq). Applying this to God: He may command faith while withholding the favor (i.e., not granting the power to believe), and if He abandons someone, He does not give them the power. This is the Ash'ari position.

Key Takeaway
God's command to believe does not obligate Him to grant the power; faith is a favor He may give or withhold.

God's Ability to Grant Success to Disbelievers
Dilemma about God's power to guide disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's ability to grant success to disbelievers would imply His incapacity, and how the dilemma leads to affirming His absolute power.

Core Idea
Denying God's power to guide anyone is blasphemy; affirming it upholds His sovereignty.

This passage presents a razor-sharp dilemma that forces opponents to either admit God's powerlessness (which is blasphemy) or confess that He is able to grant guidance to all, including disbelievers. The argument reveals that true belief in God's power requires acknowledging that He could make everyone believe if He willed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully along with the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by two conditional responses.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either Allah cannot grant success (tawfiq) to disbelievers, or He can. Consider the conclusion of each option.
The first horn leads to declaring Allah incapable, which is false; the second horn, if accepted, forces the opponent to abandon their false claim.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
This dilemma shows that denying God's ability to guide all people is inconsistent with His absolute power. The correct position is that He is able, but in His wisdom He does not force belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
211 - مسألة: ويقال لهم : هل يقدر الله على توفيق يوفق به الكافرين حتى يكونوا مؤمنين؟ فإن قالوا: لا نطقوا بتعجيز الله عز وجل، تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. وإن قالوا نعم يقدر على ذلك، ولو فعل بهم التوفيق لآمنوا، تركوا قولهم، وقالوا بالحق.
Issue 211: And it is said to them: Is Allah able to grant success (tawfiq) by which the disbelievers would become believers? If they say: No, they have spoken of Allah's incapacity (ta'jiz) – exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation. And if they say: Yes, He is able to do that, and if He did grant them success they would believe, then they have abandoned their statement and spoken the truth.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma to opponents. It asks: Is God able to grant success (tawfiq) to disbelievers so they become believers? If they say no, they declare God incapable, which is blasphemy. If they say yes, then they must admit that if God did grant success, they would believe – which contradicts their claim that God's will is not invariably enacted. The correct belief is that God is able to do so, but He does not do so by His wisdom.

Key Takeaway
God's ability to grant success to anyone is absolute; denying this would be an insult to His power, while affirming it aligns with the truth of His sovereignty.

The Power of Faith is a Grace from God
Al-Ash'ari refutes Qadariyya by arguing that faith's power is a grace, not an entitlement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that the power of faith is a divine grace, not an inherent human entitlement.

Core Idea
Faith's power is a gift from God, not a right we earn.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's Al-Ibanah tackles a central dispute with the Qadariyya: Is the ability to have faith a natural human possession or a grace from God? Through a series of logical questions, al-Ash'ari forces the opponent to admit that this ability is a grace (fadl) that God may give or withhold, establishing the foundation for the Ash'ari position on divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical structure of sequential questions.
Al-Ash'ari uses a dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the three questions
The passage contains three leading questions. Identify each one and the opponent's forced admission. They form the backbone of the argument.
The first question establishes the premise; the second defines key terms; the third applies the conclusion.
3
Understand the distinction between grace and entitlement
Al-Ash'ari clarifies that a grace (fadl) is something the giver may choose to give or withhold, unlike an entitlement (istihqaq) that must be given. This distinction is central to the argument.
This distinction refutes the Qadari claim that God must enable everyone to believe.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument defends God's justice: God may command faith while withholding the power to believe, because the command and the enablement are distinct. This is the Ash'ari position.
It prevents the conclusion that God is unjust when people disbelieve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة أخرى : ويقال لهم : أخبرونا عن قوة الإيمان أليست فضلاً من الله عز وجل؟ فلا بد من نعم . فيقال لهم : فالتفضل أليس هو ما للمتفضل أن لا يتفضل به، وله أن يتفضل به؟ فلا بد من الإجابة إلى ذلك بنعم ؛ لأن ذلك هو الفرق بين الفضل وبين الاستحقاق ويقال لهم : وللمتفضل إذا أمر بالإيمان أن يرفع التفضل ولا يتفضل بـه فيأمرهم بالإيمان، وإن خذلهم لم يعطهم قدرة الإيمان، وهذا هو قولنا ومذهبنا .
Another issue: And it is said to them: Inform us about the power of faith—is it not a grace (fadl) from God, the Mighty and Majestic? So it must be answered yes. Then it is said to them: Is not the act of bestowing grace (al-tafaddul) that which the one who bestows grace may or may not bestow? So they must answer yes to that; because that is the distinction between grace (al-fadl) and entitlement (al-istihqaq). And it is said to them: And the one who bestows grace, when he commands faith, may withhold the grace and not bestow it, but he commands them to faith, and if He lets them down (khadhalahum) He does not give them the power of faith. And this is our statement and our madhhab.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya/Mu'tazila who claimed that human power to believe is inherent, not a grace from God. Al-Ash'ari structures his argument as a series of leading questions to compel opponents to admit that the power to believe (quwwat al-iman) is a grace (fadl) from God, which God may grant or withhold. He then establishes the distinction between grace and entitlement, arguing that God, as the bestower of grace, can command faith while withholding the power to believe—thus affirming God's sovereignty without implying injustice. This is the core Ash'ari position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is unjust if He commands what is beyond human ability.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between God's command and His enablement; God can command faith without giving the power to believe, and this does not involve injustice because the power is a grace He may withhold.

Checkpoint

A) It is an entitlement
B) It is a grace
C) It is earned
D) It is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The power of faith is a gift from God that He may give or withhold; it is not an entitlement for humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Al-Ash'ari's Interpretation of 'God Does Not Will Injustice'
Al-Ash'ari interprets verses denying God's will for injustice as referring to His own acts, not human wrongs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari interprets the Quranic verses that say God does not will injustice, distinguishing between God's own actions and human actions.

Core Idea
God does not commit injustice, but He may will that humans wrong each other.

Al-Ash'ari addresses a common objection: if God does not will injustice, how can He will human disbelief, which is an injustice? In this passage, he carefully explains that the verse refers to God's own action — He does not wrong anyone — not that He prevents all injustice among humans. This distinction preserves God's omnipotence while affirming His justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the verses quoted then the interpretation.
Pay attention to the specific phrasing 'ظلمًا لهم' and 'ظلم بعضهم لبعض'.
2
Identify the key linguistic distinction
Al-Ash'ari points out that God said 'does not will injustice for them' (لهم) not 'does not will that some of them wrong others' (ظلم بعضهم لبعض). Understand why this difference matters.
The preposition 'لِ' (for/to) indicates the object of the wrong, not the agent.
3
Grasp the theological implication
Realize that the verse only denies that God Himself wrongs people. It does not deny that He wills human wrongdoing. This preserves God's absolute will over all events.
This argument defends God's justice and omnipotence simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : وإن سألوا عن قول الله عز وجل : ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ [غافر :۳۱] وعن قوله : وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ۱۰۸] . قيل لهم : معنى ذلك أنه لا يريد أن يظلمهم؛ لأنه قال: وما الله يريد ظلماً لهم، ولم يقل : لا يريد ظلم بعضهم لبعض، فلم يرد أن يظلمهم وإن كان أراد ظلم بعضهم لبعض، أي: فلم يرد أن يظلمهم وإن كان أراد أن يتظالموا .
An issue: And if they ask about the saying of God, Mighty and Majestic: 'And God does not will injustice for the servants' (40:31) and His saying: 'And God does not will injustice for the worlds' (3:108). It is said to them: The meaning of that is that He does not will to wrong them; because He said: 'And God does not will injustice for them,' and He did not say: 'He does not will that some of them wrong others.' So He did not will to wrong them, even if He willed that some of them wrong others, i.e., He did not will to wrong them, even if He willed that they wrong one another.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This is an interpretive refutation. The opponent likely argues that since God does not will injustice, He cannot have willed disbelief (which is an injustice). Al-Ash'ari shows that the verse refers to God's own action, not human actions. God does not commit injustice, but He may will that humans wrong each other. Thus the verse does not negate God's will over all events.

إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'God does not will injustice for the servants' means that God never wills any injustice to occur in the world.
Correction: The verse only means that God does not commit injustice Himself. It does not deny that He wills human wrongdoing towards each other.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what does the verse 'God does not will injustice for the servants' mean?
A) God never wills any injustice to occur
B) God does not commit injustice Himself
C) Humans are unjust by their own will independent of God
D) The verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari argues that 'God does not will injustice' means He does not wrong anyone, not that He does not will human wrongdoing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see oppression or evil in the world, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness or justice? How does this verse and Al-Ash'ari's interpretation challenge you to rethink your assumptions about God's will and human responsibility? What will you say to yourself the next time such a doubt arises?
?
If it is true that God wills even the wrong that humans do to each other, does that make you more or less careful about your own actions? How does knowing that your choices are encompassed by God's will while He remains just make you feel about accountability? Will you take more ownership of your sins knowing that God does not wrong you?
?
Reflect on a time you were wronged: Can you accept that God willed that wrong for reasons beyond your understanding, while also trusting that He never wrongs you? What would it look like this week to hold both truths—God's will over all things and His perfect justice—in your heart during a difficult situation?

God's Special Favor: Abu Bakr vs. Abu Jahl
Al-Ash'ari poses a dilemma using Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl to prove God's initial favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the concrete example of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl to prove that God's favor to believers is an initial grace, not a reward for their actions.

Core Idea
God's favor to believers is a gift from the start, not a paycheck.

Al-Ash'ari now turns from abstract argument to a concrete historical example that his opponents cannot deny: the difference between Abu Bakr, who believed, and Abu Jahl, who disbelieved. This passage forces the Mu'tazila to admit that God's distinguishing favor is initial, not earned. Let's see how the argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
2
Identify the two questions and responses
Note that Al-Ash'ari asks one question and then presents two possible answers: 'no' or 'yes'. See how each leads to a different consequence.
The two responses form a dilemma.
3
Understand the dilemma
Realize that if the opponents say 'no', they deny an obvious fact; if they say 'yes', they abandon their own doctrine. Either way, they lose.
This is a classic refutation technique.
4
Reflect on the implication
Notice that the example of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl is undeniable. Al-Ash'ari uses it to show that God's favor is not based on human action but is an initial gift.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari argument for predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب : ويقال لهم : هل تعرفون الله عز وجل نعمة على أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه خُص بها دون أبي جهل ابتداء؟ فإن قالوا: لا ، فَحُش قولُهم وإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا ،مذهبهم ، لأنهم لا يقولون : إن الله خص المؤمنين في الابتداء بما لم يخص به الكافرين
An answer: And it is said to them: Do you acknowledge that God, Mighty and Majestic, [granted] a grace to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, with which He specifically distinguished him from Abu Jahl from the beginning? If they say: No, their statement is ugly. And if they say: Yes, they have abandoned their madhhab, because they do not say that God specifically distinguished the believers from the beginning with what He did not specifically distinguish the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage uses concrete example known to all: Abu Bakr believed and Abu Jahl disbelieved. The opponents (Mu'tazila) believe that God treats all equally initially. So they cannot admit that God specially favored Abu Bakr from the beginning without abandoning their doctrine. If they deny it, the example is so clear that denial is ugly (qabih). Thus they are cornered.

B) They abandon their own madhhab
C) They agree with the Ash'aris
D) They must explain the cause

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari uses the undeniable example of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl to show that God's favor is an initial gift, not a response to human action.

Interpreting the Verse on Vain Creation
Al-Ash'ari interprets the verse about vain creation as a refutation of deniers of resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari interprets the verse 'We did not create the heaven and the earth in vain' as a specific refutation of those who deny resurrection, not as a denial of the existence of falsehood.

Core Idea
The verse's 'vain' refers to the denial of the afterlife, not to all falsehood.

Al-Ash'ari addresses an objection: if God created everything, does that include falsehood? Opponents cite the verse about creation not being in vain. In this passage, Al-Ash'ari clarifies that the verse is actually a rebuke to polytheists who rejected the resurrection. By reading the passage, you'll see how Al-Ash'ari uses context to defend divine wisdom without denying the reality of evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection presented by opponents, followed by Al-Ash'ari's response.
2
Identify the key term 'batil'
The term 'batil' (falsehood) is central. Understand how the verse's usage differs from general falsehood.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
Al-Ash'ari argues that God's statement is targeting specific deniers of the afterlife, not making a universal claim about creation.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Consider why this distinction matters for theodicy and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : وإن سألوا عن قول الله عز وجل: ﴿وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاء وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بينهما بطلا ) [ ص: ۲۷] فقالوا: هذه الآية تدل على أن الله عز وجل لم يخلق الباطل. والجواب عن ذلك : أن الله عز وجل أراد تكذيب المشركين الذين :قالوا لا حشر ولا نشور ولا إعادة، فقال تعالى: ما خلقت ذلك، وأنا لا أثيب
An issue: And if they ask about the saying of God, Mighty and Majestic: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain' (38:27). They said: This verse indicates that God, Mighty and Majestic, did not create falsehood. The answer to that is that God, Mighty and Majestic, intended to belie the polytheists who said there is no resurrection, no rising, and no recompense, so He said: I did not create that, and I will not reward.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This is another exegetical refutation. Opponents claim that if God created everything, including falsehood (batil), then His creation would be in vain. But the verse is specifically about those who deny the afterlife. God's statement is a response to them, not a general declaration that He never creates falsehood. The purpose of creation is to test and reward, so the existence of falsehood does not make creation vain.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'not created in vain' is a refutation of those who deny the afterlife, not a denial of falsehood's existence.

?
The polytheists denied the afterlife because they could not imagine bodily resurrection. What hidden doubts about Allah's power or justice do you harbor? In what area of your life do you act as if there is no final recompense? Repent and affirm your belief in the resurrection today.

The Dilemma of Initial Divine Favor
Al-Ash'ari poses a logical dilemma about initial divine favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical argument that Allah's special favor to believers is an initial act of will, not a response to merit.

Core Idea
Allah's favor to believers is by His initial will, not earned.

In this brief but powerful passage, Al-Ash'ari presents a logical dilemma to those who deny that Allah creates good and evil directly. He forces them to choose between an absurd denial of obvious divine grace and an admission that would contradict their own doctrine. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the structure is a question with two possible answers.
The words 'ابتداء' (initially) and 'تخصيص' (distinguishing) are key.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Al-Ash'ari presents two conditional responses: if they deny (No), their statement is foul; if they admit (Yes), they abandon their doctrine. Understand why each response is problematic for them.
The opponents likely hold that Allah does not distinguish between believers and disbelievers in initial creation.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this argument is effective. It uses a concrete example (Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl) to expose inconsistency in the opponent's view. What does this reveal about divine will and human merit?
This connects to debates about qadar and whether grace is earned or given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : هل تعرفون الله عز وجل نعمة على أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه خُص بها دون أبي جهل ابتداء؟
فإن قالوا: لا ، فَحُش قولُهم
وإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا مذهبهم ، لأنهم لا يقولون : إن الله خص المؤمنين في الابتداء بما لم يخص به الكافرين

If they say: No, then their statement is foul. And if they say: Yes, they abandon their doctrine, because they do not say that Allah distinguished the believers initially with what He did not distinguish the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a logical challenge (جواب) against those who deny that Allah creates good and evil directly. Al-Ash'ari poses a dilemma: Do you admit that Allah granted Abu Bakr (a believer) a special favor (like faith, guidance) that He did not give to Abu Jahl (a disbeliever) from the very beginning? If they deny this, they make an absurd statement (فَحُش قولهم) because it contradicts obvious divine grace. If they admit it, they must accept that Allah differentiates between believers and disbelievers in His initial creation and will—which goes against their presumed doctrine that Allah does not specially favor some over others in origin. Thus, they are forced to abandon their position.

Key Terms

ابتداء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'initial favor' refers only to temporal blessings like wealth or health, not to spiritual gifts like faith and guidance.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari uses 'initial favor' to mean the original granting of faith and guidance, which is a matter of divine will, not worldly blessings.

Checkpoint

B) That it was given initially and not to Abu Jahl
C) That it was the same for all believers
D) That it was only a worldly blessing

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari argues that denying Allah's initial distinction of believers is untenable; the only consistent position is that Allah's favor is by His initial will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson forces a choice: either acknowledge that your faith is a pure gift, or claim neutrality—but neutrality leads to absurdity. Which have you truly lived by? Will you thank Allah today specifically for the initial favor of faith?

Answering the Objection on Good and Evil from Allah
Al-Ash'ari answers the objection about good and evil from different sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari reconciles the verse that attributes good to Allah and evil to oneself, demonstrating it is a quotation of the polytheists and not a contradiction.

Core Idea
The apparent contradiction is resolved by recognizing the speaker.

This lesson tackles a classic predestination objection. The verse 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' seems to separate the source of good and evil. Imam al-Ash'ari reveals its context: it is a reported statement of the polytheists, and the preceding verse affirms all is from Allah. We'll explore his argument and see how the Qur'an remains consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then a detailed response using Qur'anic context.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the context of the verse
Observe how al-Ash'ari explains the meaning of 'good' and 'evil' in the verse, and how he cites the preceding verse (an-Nisa 78) to show that the phrase is actually what the polytheists said.
The key is that the verse is not Allah's own statement but a quotation.
3
Understand the resolution
Al-Ash'ari argues that the Qur'an cannot contradict itself. If one verse says all is from Allah, another cannot say the opposite. The objection is therefore invalid, and the proof is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سألوا عن قول الله عز وجل: مَا أَصَابَكَ مِنْ حَسَنَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ وَمَا أَصَابَكَ مِن سَيِّئَةٍ فَمِن نَّفْسِكَ ) [النساء : ٧٩]
والجواب عن ذلك: أن الله عز وجل قال: ﴿وَإِن تُصِبْهُمْ حَسَنَةٌ ) يعني الخصب والخير، ﴿ يَقُولُوا هَذِهِ مِنْ عِندِ اللَّهِ وَإِن تُصِبْهُمْ سَيِّئَةٌ ) يعني الجدب والقحط والمصائب، ﴿ يَقُولُواْ هَذِهِ مِنْ عِندِكَ ) أي لشؤمك، قال الله تعالى: يا محمد ( قُلْ كُلٌّ مِنْ عِندِ اللَّهِ فَمَالِ هَؤُلَاءِ الْقَوْمِ لَا يَكَادُونَ يَفْقَهُونَ حَدِيثًا ) [النساء :۷۸] في قولهم : مَا أَصَابَكَ مِنْ حَسَنَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ وَمَا أَصَابَكَ مِن سَيِّئَةٍ فَمِن نَّفْسِكَ ) فحذف قولهم؛ لأن ما تقدم من الكلام يدل عليه ؛ لأن القرآن لا يتناقض، ولا يجوز أن يقول في آية إن الكل من عند الله، ثم يقول في الآية الأخرى التي تليها إن الكل ليس من عند الله ، على أن ما أصاب الناس هو غير ما أصابوه وهذا يبين بطلان تعلقهم بهذه الآية، ويوجب عليهم الحجة
And if they ask about the saying of Allah Mighty and Majestic: 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' (an-Nisa 79). And the answer to that: That Allah Mighty and Majestic said: 'And if good befalls them' meaning plenty and prosperity, 'they say: This is from Allah. And if evil befalls them' meaning drought, famine and calamities, 'they say: This is from you' meaning because of your ill omen. Allah said: O Muhammad, 'Say: All is from Allah. So what is with these people that they hardly understand any statement?' (an-Nisa 78) regarding their saying: 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself'. So He omitted their saying because the preceding speech indicates it. For the Qur'an does not contradict, and it is not permissible that it says in one verse that all is from Allah, then says in another verse that follows it that all is not from Allah. And that what befalls people is not what they themselves do. And this clarifies the invalidity of their clinging to this verse, and it imposes the proof upon them.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage deals with a classic objection about predestination: the verse 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' (an-Nisa 79) seems to attribute evil to human agency, not to Allah. Al-Ash'ari answers by showing that this verse is actually a quotation of what the polytheists said, not Allah's own proclamation. He explains the context from the earlier verse (an-Nisa 78) where Allah rebukes the polytheists for attributing good to Allah and evil to the Prophet (as an ill omen). Allah then commands the Prophet to say 'All is from Allah'. The verse in question (79) is part of the polytheists' speech, but its wording is embedded in the narrative without explicit quotation marks (حذف قولهم). The preceding context clarifies it. Al-Ash'ari argues that the Qur'an cannot contradict itself; if one verse says all is from Allah, another cannot say otherwise. Therefore the objection fails, and the proof is established that all things, good and evil, are from Allah's creation and will.

Key Terms
حسنة
سيئة
جدب
قحط
شؤم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think an-Nisa 79 is an absolute statement by Allah teaching that evil comes from human self alone.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows it is a quotation of the disbelievers, with the preceding verse clarifying that all is from Allah.

Checkpoint
In al-Ash'ari's explanation, who is the speaker of the phrase 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself'?
A) Allah Himself
B) The Prophet Muhammad
C) The polytheists
D) The believers

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' is not a contradiction but a quotation of the polytheists; all things are from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When good things happen, do you readily thank Allah, but when trials come, do you find yourself searching for a worldly cause or blaming others? The polytheists did exactly that—attributing good to Allah and evil to the Prophet. Examine your heart: do you truly believe that all things, both good and bad, are from Allah? Are there areas in your life where you resist this belief? Make a commitment today to say 'All is from Allah' in every situation, and when hardship strikes, pause and remind yourself that this too is from Him.
?
The polytheists saw the Prophet as an ill omen, blaming him for their misfortunes. Do you ever secretly blame other people for your difficulties—your spouse, your boss, your circumstances—instead of recognizing that Allah is the source of everything? This lesson calls you to repent of such tendencies. Write down one situation where you blamed someone else and ask Allah to forgive you and to strengthen your tawhid.
?
Al-Ash'ari shows that a verse taken out of context can lead to a contradiction. When you read the Quran and encounter a verse that troubles you, do you trust that it has a valid interpretation, or do you allow doubt to creep in? This lesson demands that you seek understanding through context. Starting today, whenever you face a difficult verse, commit to reading the verses before and after before forming any opinion.

Creation for worship applies only to believers
Interprets verse 51:56 as specific to believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' is interpreted as referring specifically to believers, not all of humanity.

Core Idea
The verse about creation for worship applies only to believers, not to those destined for Hellfire.

This passage addresses a common question about the general wording of Quran 51:56. Although the verse seems to state that all jinn and humans were created for worship, the author shows that it must be restricted to believers. The key evidence is that Allah has already decreed that many will be in Hellfire, so they could not have been created for worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection and the author's answer.
The verse is 51:56, a well-known ayah about the purpose of creation.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that the verse says all jinn and humans were created to worship, but some do not worship. How can that be?
3
Understand the answer
The author answers that the verse means believers only, not disbelievers. He proves this by noting that Allah created many for Hellfire, implying they were not created for worship.
The proof uses the concept of divine decree and predestination.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this interpretation affects our understanding of God's will and human responsibility. The passage reinforces that God's purposes are specific and known to Him.
This connects to the broader theme of qadar (divine decree).

Commentary
This passage answers an objection about the verse stating that all jinn and humans were created for worship. The author argues that the verse is general but specifically applies to believers. He supports this by citing that Allah has created many for Hellfire, implying that those destined for Hell are not created for worship but for punishment. Therefore, the verse cannot include them. The author emphasizes that Allah has full knowledge and decree of who will be among the inhabitants of Hell, recording their names and lineages. Thus, the purpose of creation for worship is specific to those who will actually worship Him.

God's Obligation Despite Inability
Poses that God obligates disbelievers to listen even though they cannot hear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God can obligate disbelievers to listen to the truth even though He declares they are unable to hear, and what this implies about divine command and human ability.

Core Idea
God's command does not depend on human ability; He can obligate what He knows will not be fulfilled.

This passage raises a theological puzzle: God commands disbelievers to listen and believe, yet the Quran states they cannot hear. How can both be true? Imam al-Ash'ari uses this to show that ability is not a condition for obligation in the divine decree. Let's carefully examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the direct question-and-answer style.
2
Identify the apparent contradiction
The passage presents a question to opponents: Did God obligate disbelievers to listen and believe? Then it cites two Quranic verses stating they cannot hear. Find the tension between command and inability.
The opponents must admit both are true, creating a paradox.
3
Understand the theological point
Consider how this supports the Ash'ari view that ability is not a prerequisite for obligation. God can command what He knows will not happen, and His decree does not require enabling.
This connects to broader discussions of qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So they must answer yes. Then it is said to them: For Allah said: 'They were not able to hear' [Hud 11:20] and said: 'And they are not able to hear' [al-Kahf 18:101], and He still obligated them to listen to the truth.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage raises an issue about obligation (takleef). The author poses a question to the opponents: Did God obligate the disbelievers to listen to the truth and believe? They must answer yes. Then he points out that God also says in the Quran that they are not able to hear (e.g., Hud 11:20, al-Kahf 18:101). This seems to be a contradiction, but the author uses it to show that God can command what He knows will not be fulfilled, and that ability is not a prerequisite for obligation in this worldly trial. The unstated conclusion is that God's command does not necessitate enabling.

Key Terms

Correction: The author shows that God obligated the disbelievers to listen even though He declared them unable to hear, proving that ability is not a necessary condition for obligation.

Key Takeaway
God can command what He knows will not be done; ability is not required for obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Name one obligation you find difficult and commit to taking the first step toward fulfilling it, trusting that Allah's command is valid even when you feel unable.

The Prostration Command Refutes the Qadariyya
Uses prostration command on Judgment Day to refute Qadariyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse about prostration on Judgment Day refutes the Qadariyya claim that God must enable those He commands.

Core Idea
God can command what He does not enable; the afterlife prostration proves it.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a powerful argument from the Qur'an and hadith to refute the Qadariyya doctrine. The Qadariyya claimed that if God commands something, He must give the ability to do it. Using the scene of Judgment Day when the hypocrites are called to prostrate but cannot, the author shows that God's command does not entail enablement. This short but decisive proof cuts at the root of Qadariyya reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the rhetorical question style.
2
Identify the evidence
Find the Qur'anic verse (68:42) and the hadith cited. Understand what they describe.
The verse mentions the 'uncovering of the shin' and the call to prostrate.
3
Grasp the theological conclusion
The author concludes that God commanded prostration but did not enable the hypocrites, proving that enablement is not obligatory upon Him. This refutes the Qadariyya.
Contrast with the Qadariyya belief that command implies ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Did He not command them to prostrate in the afterlife? And it came in the report: 'That the hypocrites will have something like iron plates placed in their spines so they cannot prostrate.' And in this is confirmation of what we say: that it is not obligatory upon Allah, Mighty and Majestic, when He commands them, to enable them; and this is the invalidation of the Qadariyya's statement.

This passage provides an answer that refutes the Qadariyya sect, who claimed that God must give ability to those He commands. The author cites the verse about the Day of Judgment (68:42) where God calls people to prostrate but they cannot, and a hadith that the hypocrites will be physically unable to prostrate. This proves that God can command something without providing the ability to perform it, thus invalidating the Qadariyya doctrine that obligation implies enablement.

Key Takeaway
God's command does not obligate Him to provide ability; the prostration command on Judgment Day proves the Qadariyya wrong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Question on God Causing Pain to Children
Poses a rhetorical question using children's suffering as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses the example of children's suffering to refute the Qadariyya claim that God does not cause pain without just cause.

Core Idea
Children's suffering demonstrates God's absolute sovereignty over all pain, beyond human notions of justice.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents a pointed question to the Qadariyya: Does God not cause pain to children? By giving the example of leprosy, he challenges their assumption that pain is only a consequence of sin. This lesson examines that argument and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: it begins with 'أليس' (did not), which expects a positive answer.
This is a direct question posed to an opponent.
2
Identify the example used
The author specifically mentions leprosy (الجذام) cutting off hands and legs. Why is this example particularly powerful?
Leprosy causes visible, severe suffering in innocent children.
3
Understand the argument's force
The Qadariyya claimed God does not do evil or cause pain without reason. But here, children who cannot have sinned suffer horribly. This forces them to either admit God causes pain (contradicting their claim) or find an alternative explanation.
The question is designed to corner the opponent.
4
Reflect on the incomplete answer
The full response is not provided in this window, but the question itself stands as a strong objection. Consider how an Ash'ari might complete the argument.
This shows the importance of understanding both sides.

Answer: God Intended Believers, Not Disbelievers
Imam al-Ash'ari answers an objection about whom God intended in His address.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari defends the Ash'ari doctrine of divine decree by distinguishing between God's general address and His specific intention for believers.

Core Idea
God's address of guidance is intended only for believers, not for those He has decreed for Hell.

This passage is a direct answer to an objection raised by those who argue that since God addresses disbelievers with commands of faith, He must intend faith for them. Imam al-Ash'ari refutes this by appealing to the Quranic teaching that God has created some people for Hell and has recorded their names. He shows that God's address of guidance is intended only for those He created for worship, i.e., believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key Quranic allusion and the logical flow.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection being answered
The passage begins with 'The answer to that is…' which implies a prior objection. Consider what the objection might be: that God's address to disbelievers proves He intends faith for them.
The objection likely comes from Mu'tazila or Qadariyya.
3
Understand the argument's structure
The Imam gives a reason: because God created many for Hell and enumerated them, they are distinct from those created for worship. Therefore, God's address of guidance applies only to believers.
This establishes the Ash'ari view on divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب عن ذلك : أن الله عز وجل إنما عنى المؤمنين دون الكافرين؛ لأنه أخبرنا أنه ذرأ لجهنم كثيراً من خلقه، فالذين خلقهم لجهنم وأحصاهم وعدهم وكتبهم بأسمائهم وأسماء آبائهم وأسماء أمهاتهم غير الذين خلقهم لعبادته .
The answer to that is that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, only meant the believers, not the disbelievers, because He informed us that He has created many of His creation for Hell. Those whom He created for Hell, and enumerated them, and counted them, and wrote them by their names, the names of their fathers, and the names of their mothers, are other than those whom He created for His worship.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection (likely from the Mu'tazila or Qadariyya) who argue that God's address to disbelievers proves that God intends faith for them. The Imam answers by distinguishing between God's intention for believers and His decree for disbelievers. He references the concept that God has predestined some for Hell and recorded their names, and they are distinct from those predestined for worship (i.e., believers). This establishes the Ash'ari doctrine of divine decree (qadar).

Key Terms

Correction: Imam al-Ash'ari clarifies that God's decree for Hell is part of His wisdom, and His address of guidance is specifically for believers, not negating His justice.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari argues that God's address of guidance is specific to believers because He has decreed some for Hell and others for worship, distinguishing His intention from His general command.

?

The Justice of God in Inflicting Pain on Children
Imam al-Ash'ari argues that just as God inflicts pain on children in this world, He may do so in the next.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses an argument from analogy to defend God's justice in inflicting pain on children in the afterlife.

Core Idea
God's justice is not limited by human expectations—what is just in this world can be just in the next.

This passage presents a key dialectical argument from Imam al-Ash'ari against those who deny that God can justly inflict pain on children in the afterlife. By drawing an analogy with worldly suffering, he challenges the opponent's assumption and supports his position with prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentation style come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialogical structure: 'It is said to them...'
2
Identify the initial premise
The Imam starts by establishing a premise that even opponents accept: God inflicts pain on children in this world (e.g., leprosy) and that is considered just. Find this first move in the text.
This is the foundation of the analogy.
3
Follow the opponent's objection and Imam's rebuttal
The opponent argues that worldly pain is for admonition of parents. The Imam then extends the analogy: if that purpose is just, then inflicting pain on children of disbelievers in the afterlife to anger their parents would also be just. Trace this reasoning.
Note the shift in purpose: 'admonition' vs. 'anger'.
4
Examine the hadith support
The Imam cites two hadiths: one about the fire test for children of polytheists, and another about hearing their crying. Consider how these support his argument that God's actions in the afterlife are just and purposeful.
The hadiths indicate a test, not arbitrary punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۲۰ - مسألة في إيلام الأطفال : ويقال لهم : أليس قد آلم الله عز وجل الأطفال في الدنيا بآلام أوصلها إليهم؟ كنحو الجذام الذي يقطع أيديهم وأرجلهم، وغير ذلك مما يؤلمهم به، وكان ذلك سائغاً جائزاً. فإذا قالوا: نعم . قيل لهم : فإذا كان هذا عدلاً فما أنكرتم أن يؤلمهم في الآخرة ويكون ذلك منه عدلاً . فإن قالوا آلمهم في الدنيا ليعتبر بهم الآباء . قيل لهم : فإذا فعل بهم ذلك في الدنيا ليعتبر بهم الآباء، وكان ذلك منه عدلاً فلم لا يؤلم أطفال الكافرين في الآخرة ليغيظ بذلك آباءهم، ويكون ذلك منه عدلاً؟ وقد قيل في الخبر : ( إِنَّ أَطْفَالَ المُشْرِكِينَ تُؤَجَّجُ لَهُمْ نَارٌ يَوْمَ القِيامَة، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ لَهُمْ: اقْتَحِمُوهَا ، فَمَنْ اقْتَحَمَهَا أَدْخَلَهُ الجَنَّةَ، وَمَنْ لَمْ يَقْتَحِمْهَا أَدْخَلَهُ النَّارَ) . وقد قيل في الأطفال، وروي عن النبي : ( إِن شئت أسْمَعْتُك صَغَاءَهُم في النَّارِ) .
Issue concerning inflicting pain on children: And it is said to them: Did not Allah, Mighty and Majestic, inflict pain on children in this world with pains He caused to reach them? Such as leprosy that cuts off their hands and legs, and other things that pain them, and that was permissible and lawful. If they say: Yes, it is said to them: Since this is justice, why do you deny that He may inflict pain on them in the Hereafter and that be justice from Him? If they say: He inflicted pain on them in this world so that fathers may take heed. It is said to them: If He did that to them in this world so that fathers may take heed, and that was justice from Him, then why does He not inflict pain on the children of the disbelievers in the Hereafter to anger thereby their fathers, and that be justice from Him? And it has been said in the report: 'Indeed the children of the polytheists will have a fire kindled for them on the Day of Resurrection, then it will be said to them: Jump into it. Whoever jumps into it, He will admit him to Paradise, and whoever does not jump, He will admit him to the Fire.' And it has been said about children, and it is narrated from the Prophet: 'If you wish, I will make you hear their crying in the Fire.'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the justice of God in inflicting pain on children. The opponents (likely Mu'tazila) argue that it is unjust for God to punish children in the afterlife. The Ash'ari responds by pointing out that God already inflicts pain on children in this world (e.g., leprosy), and people consider that just. So why cannot He do so in the afterlife? They might argue that worldly pain is for admonition of parents. The Ash'ari then extends the analogy: if that is just, then inflicting pain on the children of disbelievers in the afterlife to anger their parents would also be just. He supports this with hadiths about the fire test for children of polytheists and about their crying.

Key Terms
إيلام
الجذام
اعتبار
أطفال المشركين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Imam al-Ash'ari asserts that children will be arbitrarily punished in the afterlife.
Correction: The Imam argues from analogy that it is just for God to do so, and cites a hadith showing a test, not arbitrary punishment. His point is about the consistency of divine justice.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Imam al-Ash'ari use to argue that God may inflict pain on children in the afterlife?
A) God inflicts pain on children in this world.
B) God rewards children in this world.
C) Parents are punished instead.
D) Children are never punished.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari defends the justice of God's actions by showing that what is just in this world cannot be unjust in the next.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a child suffering in this world, do you ever feel that God is unjust? How does this lesson expose the hidden assumption that you know what God owes His creation? Will you repent of that presumption and submit to His absolute wisdom?
?
If God can inflict pain on innocent children for a purpose beyond your understanding, what makes you think your own trials are meaningless or unfair? What would it mean for you to trust God's justice even when your heart screams 'why'? Are you ready to say 'He does as He wills' without reservation?
?
The hadith mentions children being commanded to jump into fire as a test. What 'fire' is God commanding you to enter right now—a difficult relationship, a hard truth, a sacrifice? Are you hesitating because it seems unjust, or will you jump in obedience, trusting that His command is just even if you cannot see the outcome?

The Dilemma of God's Command and Foreknowledge
Imam Ash'ari confronts the Mu'tazila with a dilemma about God's command to those He knows will not believe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Imam Ash'ari uses to demonstrate the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite position on free will and divine foreknowledge.

Core Idea
God's command to the non-believer forces a choice between blasphemy and conceding Ash'arite doctrine.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents a crisp logical trap for the Mu'tazilite position on free will. The question seems simple: Does God command belief from those He knows will never believe? But the implications are devastating. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and then the translation. Notice the structure of the dilemma: a question, two possible answers, and the consequences of each.
Pay attention to the logic: if they say No, they agree with us; if Yes, they blaspheme.
2
Identify the two branches
The passage presents two possible answers from the Mu'tazila: 'Yes' or 'No'. Each leads to a different problem for them. Identify the consequences of each.
3
Understand the trap
If they say No, they contradict the Qur'an (since God clearly commands belief). If they say Yes, they imply humans can escape God's knowledge. Both are unacceptable for the Mu'tazila.
This dilemma exposes the incoherence of their view.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
This argument shows that affirming divine foreknowledge does not conflict with God's command, and that human free will cannot override God's knowledge.
This is a key Ash'arite proof for predestination within the framework of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۲۲- مسألة : ويقال لهم : أليس أمر الله عز وجل بالإيمان من علم أنه لا يؤمن؟ فمن قولهم: نعم، يقال لهم : فأنتم قادرون على الإيمان، ويتأتى لكم وإن قالوا: لا، وافقوا. وإن قالوا: نعم، زعموا أن العباد يقدرون على الخروج من علم الله تعالى الله عز وجل عن ذلك علواً كبيراً .
222 - Issue: And it is said to them: Did not Allah, Mighty and Majestic, command belief from those whom He knows will not believe? If they say: Yes, it is said to them: Then you are capable of faith, and it is attainable for you. And if they say: No, they agree [with us]. And if they say: Yes, they claim that servants are able to escape Allah's knowledge; Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is far above that, greatly exalted.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a logical dilemma posed to the Mu'tazila. The question is: Does Almighty God command belief from those whom He knows will not believe? The opponent has two possible answers, both problematic. If they answer 'No,' they agree with the Ash'arite position that God does command such people (since the Qur'an explicitly commands belief even from those who will not). If they answer 'Yes,' then they are trapped into claiming that human beings have the power to escape God's foreknowledge, implying that God's knowledge can be falsified. This is blasphemous. Thus, the Mu'tazila are forced into either conceding the Ash'arite view or uttering a statement that exalts human power over divine knowledge. The argument demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite position on human free will leads to a contradiction with God's omniscience.

Key Terms
أمر
علم الله
قدرة العباد
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can freely choose to believe or not independently of God's knowledge; God's knowledge does not constrain their actions.
Correction: If God knows that someone will not believe, then that knowledge is true and cannot be falsified. Thus, human actions cannot escape divine foreknowledge; claiming otherwise is blasphemous.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila answer 'Yes' to the question?
A) They affirm that God does not command belief.
B) They imply that humans can escape God's knowledge.
C) They concede that God's command is not binding.
D) They prove that humans are not responsible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position on free will leads to the absurd conclusion that humans can overcome God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever act as if your choices are hidden from Allah's knowledge? How does knowing that He knows all—including your disobedience—affect your repentance and your view of His mercy?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that humans can create their own actions independently of Allah's foreknowledge. Where in your life do you subtly assume your choices are outside His control? What would it mean to surrender that illusion?
?
If Allah commands you even knowing you will fail, does that make His command less serious? How does this truth press you to take His commands more seriously, not less, and to rely on His help to obey?

True Meaning of Qadari: Attributing Power to Oneself
Al-Ash'ari defines Qadari using linguistic analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari redefines the term 'Qadari' based on linguistic usage to refute the Qadariyya's claim.

Core Idea
A Qadari is one who attributes power to himself, not to God.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a key dispute over the label 'Qadari'. The Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) claimed that since Ash'aris affirm God's predestination of evil, they should be called Qadari. Al-Ash'ari turns the tables by defining the term linguistically: a Qadari is one who claims power over his own actions apart from God, not one who affirms divine qadr. He uses everyday examples of a jeweler and a carpenter to illustrate this point, showing that the real Qadariyya are those who deny divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a definition, examples, and application.
Focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the definition of Qadari
Identify the key definition: 'القدري هو من يثبت القدر لنفسه دون ربه'. This is the core of al-Ash'ari's argument.
Compare with the opponents' definition.
3
Analyze the linguistic analogy
Examine the examples of the jeweler (صائغ) and carpenter (نجار). How do they show that the name applies to the one who claims action as his own, not the one who attributes it to another?
This analogy is central to the rebuttal.
4
Apply to the dispute
See how al-Ash'ari applies this logic: since the Qadariyya claim they do actions without God, they are the true Qadariyya. The Ash'aris say actions are made possible by God, so they are not.
This refutes the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٢٤ - مسألة : وزعمت القدرية أنا نستحق اسم القدر ؛ لأنا نقول: إن الله عز وجل قدر الشر والكفر، فمن يثبت القدر كان قدرياً دون من لم يثبته فيقال لهم : القدري هو من يثبت القدر لنفسه دون ربه عز وجل، وأنه يقدر أفعاله دون خالقه، وكذلك هو في اللغة؛ لأن الصائغ هو من زعم أنه يصوغ دون من يقول إنه يصاغ له، والنجار هو من يضيف النجارة إلى نفسه دون من يزعم أنه ينجر له، فلما كنتم تزعمون أنكم تقدرون أعمالكم وتفعلونها دون ربكم وجب أن تكونوا قدرية ولم نكن نحن قدرية ؛ لأنا لم نضف الأعمال إلى أنفسنا دون ربنا عز وجل، ولم نقل إنا نقدرها دونه، وقلنا: إنها تقدر لنا .
224 - Issue: And the Qadariyya claimed that we deserve the name 'Qadari' because we say: Allah Almighty measured (qaddara) evil and disbelief, so whoever affirms qadr is a Qadari, not whoever does not affirm it. So it is said to them: A Qadari is he who affirms qadr for himself without his Lord Almighty, and that he is able to perform his actions without his Creator. And similarly it is in the language: because the jeweler (al-sa'igh) is he who claims that he fashions (yasughu) without the one who says that it is fashioned for him, and the carpenter (al-najjar) is he who attributes carpentry to himself without the one who claims that it is carpentered for him. So when you claim that you are able to do your actions and you do them without your Lord, it becomes necessary that you be Qadari. And we were not Qadari because we did not attribute actions to ourselves without our Lord Almighty, nor did we say that we are able to perform them without Him, and we said: they are made possible for us (tuqaddaru lana).
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definition and rebuttal. The Qadariyya (here the Mu'tazila) claim that the Ash'ari are the true Qadariyya because they affirm God's predestination of evil. Al-Ash'ari responds by defining 'Qadari' linguistically: it is one who attributes power to himself, not to his Lord. He gives two examples: a jeweler (sa'igh) is one who claims the act of jewelry-making as his own, not one who says it is done for him. Similarly, a carpenter is one who attributes carpentry to himself. Likewise, the Ash'ari argue that since the Qadariyya claim they can perform their actions independently of God, they truly deserve the name, whereas the Ash'ari attribute all power and actions to God and say actions are made possible for them by God.

Key Terms

الصائغ
النجار
القدر
فعل العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that affirming God's predestination of evil makes one a Qadari.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the name Qadari applies to one who claims independent power for himself, not one who affirms divine qadr.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, who truly deserves the name Qadari?
A) Whoever affirms God's predestination of all things.
B) Whoever claims to have power over his actions independently of God.
C) Whoever attributes actions to God.
D) Whoever denies human ability.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari redefines 'Qadari' linguistically to mean one who claims independent power, thereby refuting the Qadariyya's accusation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent success or achievement. In your heart, did you attribute it to your own ability and effort, or did you truly acknowledge that Allah made it possible for you? How would your attitude change if you consistently said, 'It was made possible for me' rather than 'I did it'?
?
When you encounter a failure or a situation beyond your control, do you become frustrated as if you should have been able to overcome it on your own? How can the recognition that all power belongs to Allah reshape your response to difficulty starting today?
?
The analogy of the jeweler and carpenter exposes the pride of claiming independent action. Is there an area of your life—your work, your worship, your relationships—where you subtly act as if you are the independent agent? What one concrete step can you take this week to remind yourself that even your smallest actions are only made possible by Allah?
