Rebuttal: Affirming God's Qadr Entails Qadari
Al-Ash'ari argues that if affirming qadr makes one Qadari, then opponents must also be Qadari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that affirming predestination makes one a Qadari.

Core Idea
If affirming qadr makes you Qadari, then the opponents themselves are Qadari.

This passage presents a sharp logical rebuttal. Al-Ash'ari turns the opponents' own criterion against them, showing that their accusation is self-defeating. By examining this concise argument, we see the power of consistent reasoning in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that affirming qadr makes one Qadari. Find this claim in the passage.
The phrase 'إذا كان من أثبت التقدير الله عز وجل قدرياً' contains the opponent's premise.
3
Analyze Al-Ash'ari's counter
Al-Ash'ari applies the same criterion to the opponents: they affirm qadr for heavens, earth, and acts of obedience. So by their own rule, they are Qadari. Since they reject this, their rule is inconsistent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's premise leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٢٥- جواب : ويقال لهم : إذا كان من أثبت التقدير الله عز وجل قدرياً، فيلزمكم إذا زعمتم أن الله عز وجل قدر السموات والأرض وقدر الطاعات أن تكونوا قدرية، فإذا لم يلزم هذا فقد بطل قولكم وانتقض كلامكم
225 - Answer: And it is said to them: If the one who affirms predestination (al-taqdir) to Allah Almighty is a Qadari, then it would be necessary upon you, when you claim that Allah Almighty predestined the heavens and the earth and predestined acts of obedience, that you be Qadari. So if this does not follow, then your statement is nullified and your speech is contradicted.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This is a brief logical rebuttal. The opponent claimed that affirming qadr makes one Qadari. Al-Ash'ari applies this to the opponent themselves: they affirm that God predestined the heavens, earth, and acts of obedience. By their own criterion, they would be Qadari. But they reject that label. Therefore their criterion is inconsistent—it does not apply to them, so it is invalid. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
التقدير
قدري
السموات والأرض
الطاعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming God's predestination (qadr) makes one a Qadari.
Correction: The opponents themselves affirm God's predestination of heavens, earth, and acts of obedience, so by their own standard they would be Qadari. Thus the accusation is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What flaw does al-Ash'ari expose in the opponent's claim about who is Qadari?
A) The opponent's definition is too broad.
B) The opponent's own belief in God's predestination of heavens and earth would make them Qadari by their own rule.
C) The opponent denies God's power.
D) The opponent agrees with al-Ash'ari.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that a theological accusation must apply consistently to all parties, including the accuser.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accuse someone of a theological error, do you ever stop to check whether you yourself might be committing the same error? What is at stake in avoiding that self-examination?
?
This lesson shows that a flawed premise leads to contradiction. In your own beliefs, are you willing to trace your premises to their logical conclusions, even if it means you must change your mind? Or do you hold some beliefs simply out of habit or comfort?
?
Al-Ash'ari's argument is sharp and direct. Think of a time you avoided a hard logical confrontation about your faith. What was the fear that held you back, and how can you combat it in the future?

God Creates Disbelief: Refuting the Objection
Refutation that God cannot both guide and seal hearts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that God cannot both explain and seal hearts, and concludes that God creates disbelief.

Core Idea
God's sealing of hearts is His direct creation, not a response to human choice.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari defends the doctrine of divine predestination (qadar) against the objection that God cannot simultaneously explain (guide) and seal (misguide) a heart. He argues that the same logic would prevent guidance and misguidance from coexisting, which they do. Through Quranic evidence, he establishes that God actively creates the inability to believe, thereby creating disbelief itself. This lesson unpacks his argument and its scriptural foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: objection, reductio, Quranic evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the objection and the reply
Find where the opponent states that explanation and sealing cannot coexist. Then see how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum to show the inconsistency.
The author equates the opponent's logic to forbidding guidance and misguidance together, which is false.
3
Analyze the Quranic citations
Examine the verses from Surah Yunus and Surah Fussilat. Understand how each supports the point that God actively seals hearts and creates the inability to believe.
Moses' prayer asks for hardening, and God answers it. The disbelievers themselves admit to coverings.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final statement: if God creates constriction, that constriction is disbelief itself. This shows God creates disbelief and sins.
This aligns with the Ash'ari view of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع الشرح، والضيق مع السعة، والهدى مع الضلال؟ إن كان هذا جائزاً، جاز أن يجتمع التوحيد والإلحاد الذي هو ضد التوحيد، والكفر والإيمان معاً في قلب واحد، وإن لم يجز هذا لم يجز ما قلتموه.
فإن قالوا: الختم والضيق والضلال لا يجوز أن يجتمع مع شرح الله
قيل لهم : وكذلك الهدى لا يجتمع مع الضلال، وإذا كان هكذا فما شرح الله صدور الكافرين للإيمان، بل ختم الله على قلوبهم، وأقفلهـا عـن الحق وشد عليها كما دعا نبي الله موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام على قومه فقال: ﴿رَبَّنَا أَطْمِس عَلَى أَمْوَالِهِمْ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا حَتَّى يَرَوُا الْعَذَابَ الأليم ﴾ [يونس : ۸۸] ، وقال الله عز وجل: ﴿ قَدْ أُجِيبَت دَعْوَتُكُمَا ﴾ [يونس : ۸۹] وقال عز وجل يخبر عن الكافرين أنهم قالوا قُلُوبُنَا فِي أَكِنَةٍ مِّمَّا تَدْعُونَا إِلَيْهِ وَفِي عَاذَانِنَا وَقَرٌ وَمِنْ بَيْنِنَا وَبَيْنكَ حِجَابٌ [فصلت: ٥] فإذا خلق الله الأكنة في قلوبهم والقفل والزيغ والختم وضيق الصدر، ثم أمرهم بالإيمان الذي علم أنه لا يكون، فقد أمرهم بما لا يقدرون عليه، وإذا خلق الله في قلوبهم ما ذكرناه من الضيق عن الإيمان، فهل الضيق عن الإيمان إلا الكفر الذي في قلوبهم، وهذا يبين أن الله خلق كفرهم ومعاصيهم .
Is explanation and constriction, expansion and tightness, guidance and misguidance? If this is permissible, then it would be permissible for tawheed and ilhad (which is the opposite of tawheed) and disbelief and faith to coexist together in a single heart. And if this is not permissible, then what you have said is not permissible. If they say: Sealing, constriction, and misguidance cannot coexist with God's explanation. It is said to them: Likewise guidance does not coexist with misguidance. And if this is so, then God did not explain the hearts of the disbelievers for faith, but rather God sealed their hearts, and locked them from the truth and tightened them, as the Prophet of God Moses (peace be upon him) supplicated against his people, saying: 'Our Lord, obliterate their wealth and harden their hearts so that they will not believe until they see the painful punishment' [Yunus: 88]. And God Almighty said: 'Your supplication has been answered' [Yunus: 89]. And God Almighty informed about the disbelievers that they said: 'Our hearts are in coverings from what you call us to, and in our ears is deafness, and between us and you is a barrier' [Fussilat: 5]. So if God creates coverings in their hearts, and the lock, deviation, sealing, and constriction of the chest, then commands them to faith which He knows will not occur, then He has commanded them with what they are unable to do. And if God creates in their hearts what we have mentioned of constriction from faith, then is constriction from faith anything other than the disbelief which is in their hearts? And this shows that God created their disbelief and sins.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent claims that God cannot simultaneously perform explanation (expansion of the chest for faith) and sealing (constriction) in a heart. The author refutes this by showing the same logic would prevent guidance and misguidance from coexisting, but they do. Then the author cites Quranic verses to prove that God actively seals hearts, creating the inability to believe. The conclusion is that God creates disbelief and sins, defending predestination (qadar) against the charge of injustice.

ختم
هدى

كفر
إيمان
قفل
زيغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's sealing of hearts is merely a response to human choice, not a direct creation.
Correction: The author argues that God directly creates the sealing, coverings, and constriction in hearts, as shown by Moses' prayer and the disbelievers' description, and concludes that God creates disbelief.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the argument about combining opposite states?
A) God does not create disbelief.
B) God creates both faith and disbelief in hearts.
C) Human freedom prevents God from sealing hearts.
D) The objection proves that God is unjust.

Key Takeaway
God directly creates disbelief in the hearts of disbelievers, as evidenced by His sealing and hardening of their hearts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Oaths, Exception, and Divine Will
Ash'ari argues from oath exception to divine omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the use of 'if God wills' in oaths demonstrates the effectiveness of divine will, and why failing to fulfill an oath with this exception does not constitute perjury.

Core Idea
The exception 'if God wills' ties the oath's fulfillment to God's actual decree.

This passage uses a concrete legal scenario—a man swearing to repay a debt with 'if God wills'—to argue a profound theological point about divine sovereignty. Imam Ash'ari shows that the very structure of such an oath presupposes that God's will is determinative. By analyzing whether the oath-taker is guilty of perjury when he fails, we uncover a rational proof for the Ash'ari doctrine that nothing occurs except by God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: a question, an expected answer, and an analogy.
The argument is concise and legal in tone.
2
Identify the hypothetical and the opponent's concession
Find the hypothetical scenario: a man swears to pay a debt 'if God wills'. The opponent is forced to admit that God wills the payment. Then note the consequence: if the man fails, he is not perjured because the condition was not fulfilled.
The logic hinges on the condition being a genuine expression of divine will.
3
Analyze the analogy with the unconditional oath
Compare with the oath tied to dawn: if he swears 'at dawn' and dawn comes, he must pay or be perjured. The difference shows that 'if God wills' is not merely a pious phrase but has real legal effect, confirming that God's will determines outcomes.
This underscores the Ash'ari view that no event occurs without God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الاستثناء : ويقال لهم خبرونا عن مطالبة رجل بحق فقال له : والله لأعطينك ذلك غداً إن شاء الله تعالى أليس الله شاء أن يعطيه حقه؟ فإن قالوا: نعم . يقال لهم: أفرأيتم إن جاء الغد فلم يعطه حقه، أليس لا يحنث ؟ فلا بد من نعم . فيقال لهم: فلو كان الله شاء أن يعطيه حقه لحنث إذا لم يعطه، كما لو قال: والله لأعطينك حقك إذا طلع الفجر غداً، ثم طلـع ولم يعطه يكون حانثاً .
Issue regarding exception (istithna): And it is said to them: Inform us about a man demanding a right, and he said to him: 'By Allah, I will surely give you that tomorrow, if God wills.' Does God not will that he gives him his right? If they say yes. It is said to them: Do you not see that if tomorrow comes and he does not give him his right, is he not then breaking his oath? There is no avoiding 'yes'. So it is said to them: If God had willed that he gives him his right, he would have broken his oath when he did not give it, just as if he said: 'By Allah, I will give you your right when dawn breaks tomorrow,' then dawn breaks and he does not give it, he would be breaking his oath.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theological argument about oaths and divine will. The author uses a hypothetical: a man swears to pay a debt 'if God wills'. The opponent must admit that God's will is for the debt to be paid. If the man fails to pay, he is not perjured because the condition ('if God wills') was not fulfilled. This shows that God's will is effective; if God had willed the payment, the man would have paid (and would be guilty of perjury if he did not). The analogy with an unconditional oath (at dawn) confirms that the exception clause changes the ruling. The underlying point is that God's will (mashi'ah) determines all occurrences, consistent with the Ashari doctrine of predestination.

Key Terms
استثناء (exception)
يمين (oath)
حنث (perjury)
مشيئة (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that saying 'if God wills' is merely a pious formula with no real effect on the oath's validity.
Correction: This passage shows that 'if God wills' is a genuine condition that determines whether the oath is binding. If the condition is not met (God does not will the action), the oath-taker is not guilty of perjury, proving that God's will is determinative.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a man who swears 'by Allah, I will give you your right tomorrow if God wills' not break his oath if he fails to pay?
A) Because the debt was not legally due.
B) Because the condition 'if God wills' was not fulfilled; God did not will the payment.
C) Because oaths with exception are invalid in Islamic law.
D) Because the man intended to lie from the start.

Key Takeaway
The exception 'if God wills' in oaths shows that divine will is not a passive wish but the actual determinant of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'insha'Allah' for your plans, do you truly live as if the outcome is in God's hands, or is it just a habit? How would your anxiety change if you fully internalized that nothing occurs without His will?
?
Consider a promise you made recently. Did you attach it to God's will, or did you rely on your own ability? How does this passage call you to examine your sense of control?
?
If you truly believed that every event, even your failures, are by God's will, how would that change your reaction to unmet expectations? This lesson argues that the exception clause is not empty—are your own 'if God wills' words empty or heartfelt?

The Argument from Conditional Oaths Against the Qadariyya
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Qadariyya using the conditional oath (istithna').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses the legal ruling on conditional oaths to refute the Qadariyya position that Allah wills all actions.

Core Idea
A broken oath disproves divine will for the forbidden act.

Al-Ash'ari presents a clever dialectical argument against the Qadariyya (those who claim humans create their own actions). He takes a common scenario—a man swears to repay a debt 'if Allah wills'—and shows that the legal conclusion (no perjury when he fails) implies that Allah did not will the payment. This refutes the Qadariyya claim that Allah wills all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical structure: the author poses questions to the opponent.
2
Identify the two key questions
The passage contains two crucial questions posed to the Qadariyya: (1) Did Allah will that the man give the right? (2) Does the man break his oath when he fails to give?
Understand the expected answers: yes to the first, no to the second.
3
Understand the logical implication
The argument proceeds: if Allah had willed the payment, then failure to pay would be a sin (perjury). But the oath was conditional, so no perjury occurs. Therefore, Allah did not will the payment.
This contradicts the Qadariyya belief that Allah wills all human actions.
4
Connect to the refutation of Qadariyya
The Qadariyya claim that humans create their own actions and that Allah wills all actions. Al-Ash'ari shows that if Allah willed an act, its omission would be a sin, but in the conditional oath, omission is not a sin, so Allah did not will that act.
This undermines the Qadariyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم خبرونا عن مطالبة رجل بحق فقال له : والله لأعطينك ذلك غداً إن شاء الله تعالى أليس الله شاء أن يعطيه حقه؟ فإن قالوا: نعم . يقال لهم: أفرأيتم إن جاء الغد فلم يعطه حقه، أليس لا يحنث ؟ فلا بد من نعم . فيقال لهم: فلو كان الله شاء أن يعطيه حقه لحنث إذا لم يعطه، كما لو قال: والله لأعطينك حقك إذا طلع الفجر غداً، ثم طلع ولم يعطه يكون حانثاً .
And we say to them: tell us about a man demanding his right, and he said to him: 'By Allah, I will give you that tomorrow, if Allah wills.' Did Allah will that he give him his right? If they say: Yes. We say to them: Do you see that if tomorrow comes and he does not give him his right, does he not break his oath? There must be yes. Then we say to them: So if Allah had willed that he give him his right, he would have broken his oath when he did not give it, just as if he said: 'By Allah, I will give you your right when dawn breaks tomorrow,' then dawn broke and he did not give, he would be breaking his oath.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical proof against the Qadariyya concerning the implication of the exception 'if Allah wills' (istithna'). The argument proceeds in steps: First, the opponent is asked about a man who swears to pay a debt 'tomorrow, if Allah wills.' Does Allah will that he pay? The opponent must say yes, because Allah wills obedience. Second, if tomorrow comes and he does not pay, is he guilty of perjury? The opponent must say no, because the oath was conditioned on Allah's will. Third, the key point: if Allah had truly willed the payment, the man would be guilty of perjury for not paying, but since he is not guilty, it follows that Allah did not will the payment. The analogy with an unconditional oath confirms that the absence of guilt disproves divine will for the act.

Key Terms
الاستثناء
الحنث
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'if Allah wills' makes the oath automatically fulfilled or that it is a mere formality without legal effect.
Correction: The passage shows that 'if Allah wills' is a genuine condition that nullifies the obligation and prevents perjury if the condition is not met, revealing that Allah's will is not necessarily realized in the act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, why does the man not break his oath when he fails to give the right after swearing 'if Allah wills'?
A) Because his intention was not to give.
B) Because the oath was conditioned on Allah's will, and since he did not give, Allah must not have willed it.
C) Because the oath is invalid from the beginning.
D) Because he forgot to give.

Key Takeaway
Conditional oaths reveal that Allah's will is not necessarily fulfilled in human actions, refuting the Qadariyya claim that Allah wills all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'if Allah wills' before a plan, is it a genuine submission to His will or a thoughtless habit? Today, identify one area where you have been insisting on your own plan without that submission, and actively pray for Allah's will to prevail.
?
Have you ever sworn to do something and later failed to do it? Did you feel guilt or did you remember that you said 'if Allah wills'? How does that realization change your view of Allah's control over your actions? Commit to reviewing your oaths and intentions with this lesson in mind.
?
If you truly believed that every unfulfilled intention is a sign that Allah did not will it, how would your disappointment or frustration transform into contentment? Choose one recent disappointment and reframe it as a divine permission, then thank Allah for preventing what He did not will for you.

The Fixed Term of the Murdered
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Qadariyya on the term of the murdered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the term of the murdered person is fixed and cannot be based on hypotheticals.

Core Idea
The Qur'an fixes every soul's term; the murdered die at their appointed time, not before.

This passage from Al-Ash'ari's Al-Ibanah presents a powerful scriptural and logical argument against the Qadariyya who claimed that some deaths occur before their term. By citing two clear Qur'anic verses and posing a dilemma, Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that the term of the murdered is fixed and that any other view contradicts revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qur'anic verses
Read the two verses that establish that no term can be advanced or delayed. Notice the absolute language.
These verses are the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the dilemma
Al-Ash'ari asks: was the murdered person killed at his term or not? Consider both answers and their consequences.
If yes, the Qadariyya concede the point. If no, they must define the term.
3
Examine the hypothetical definition
The Qadariyya might define the term as a time when hypothetical events would have occurred. See why this is absurd and contradicts the verses.
This definition is unverifiable and conflicts with the fixed nature of the term.
4
Conclude the argument
The only consistent position is that the term is fixed and known only to Allah. The murdered died at his appointed time.
This upholds divine decree and the clarity of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : أليس قد قال الله عز وجل: فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةٌ وَلَا يستقدِمُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ٣٤] وقال: ﴿وَلَن يُؤَخِّرَ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهَا) [ المنافقون : ١١] ؟ فلا بد من نعم . يقال لهم: فخبرونا عمن قتله قاتل ظُلماً، أتزعمون أنه قتل في أجله أو بغير أجله؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، وافقوا وقالوا بالحق، وتركوا القدر . وإن قالوا: لا . قيل لهم: فمتى أجل هذا المقتول؟ فإن قالوا: الوقت الذي علم الله أنه لو لم يقتل لتزوج امرأة علم أنها امرأته، وإن لم يبلغ إلى أن يتزوجها، وإذا كان في معلوم الله أنه لو لم يقتل وبقي لكفر أن تكون النار داره ، وإذا لم يجز هذا لم يجز أن يكون الوقت الذي لم يبلغ إليه أجلاً له على أن هذا القول لا مقيد لقول الله عز وجل: فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةٌ وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ٣٤]
We say to them: Did not Allah Almighty say: 'And when their term comes, they cannot postpone it an hour nor advance it' [7:34] and He said: 'And Allah will not postpone a soul when its term comes' [63:11]? There must be yes. We say to them: Tell us about the one whom a murderer killed unjustly: do you claim that he was killed at his term or not at his term? If they say: Yes, they agree and speak the truth, and abandon Qadar. And if they say: No. We say to them: When is the term of this murdered one? If they say: The time that Allah knew that if he had not been killed, he would have married a woman that He knew was his wife, even if he did not reach marrying her; and if it is in Allah's knowledge that if he had not been killed and remained, he would have disbelieved so that the Fire would be his abode. And if this is not permissible, then it is not permissible that the time which he did not reach be his term; moreover this statement does not restrict the words of Allah Almighty: 'And when their term comes, they cannot postpone it an hour nor advance it' [7:34].
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya using Qur'anic verses on the fixed term (ajal). Al-Ash'ari first cites two verses that clearly state that no soul can be advanced or delayed when its term comes. Then he asks the opponent about a murdered person: was he killed at his term or not? If they answer 'yes,' they concede the point and abandon Qadariyya. If they answer 'no,' then they must specify when his term actually was. Their hypothetical answer (a time when, had he lived, he would have married or disbelieved) is absurd because it defines the term retroactively by hypotheticals that never occurred. Moreover, such a definition contradicts the clear meaning of the Qur'anic verses, which do not allow a term to be relative to unfulfilled events.

Key Terms
الأجل
القدر
القاتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The murdered person died before his term.
Correction: The Qur'an states that no soul dies before its term; therefore, the murdered person died at his fixed term, not before.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari argue is the problem with the Qadariyya's definition of the murdered person's term?
A) It contradicts the Qur'anic assertion that terms are fixed and cannot be changed.
B) It relies on a person's free will.
C) It denies divine knowledge.
D) It makes the term depend on the murderer's action.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument proves that the term of every soul, including the murdered, is fixed and known only to Allah, and cannot be defined by hypothetical events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that when you hear of a tragic death—a murder, an accident, a sudden illness—that person's term had come exactly as Allah decreed, or do you secretly question the wisdom or justice of that timing? How would this conviction change your grief and your trust in Allah?
?
If you were to die tomorrow, would you be content that your term is fixed and known only to Allah, or does the thought of an 'untimely' death unsettle you? What does that reveal about your reliance on divine decree?
?
When you face a threat or danger, does your heart rest in the knowledge that your term cannot be advanced or delayed, or do you panic as if you could outrun Allah's decree? How can you cultivate that certainty in your daily life?

Human Control Over Appointed Terms Is Heresy
Al-Ash'ari argues that Qadariyya belief leads to heresy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the Qadariyya belief that humans have control over appointed terms.

Core Idea
If humans control life and death, they usurp Allah's exclusive prerogative — that is heresy.

This passage is a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Qadariyya, who affirm human free will over actions. Imam al-Ash'ari shows that their logic logically leads to attributing divine control over life and death to humans, which is indefensible. Let's see how he constructs his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure and the series of consequences.
Notice the repetition of 'qadir' (able) and the emphasis on ability.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The argument starts with 'If the murderer is able not to kill...' (if-quadir). Trace how each consequence flows from that premise.
3
Analyze the logical implications
From the murderer's ability not to kill, al-Ash'ari derives three powers: to cut short the term, to delay it, and ultimately to control all terms. List these implications.
These are enumerated in the Arabic: 'قطع أجله', 'تأخيره', 'يقدم آجال العباد ويؤخرها'.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how al-Ash'ari generalizes from the murderer to all humans, then declares this position 'heresy' (ilhad).
Heresy here means attributing divine attributes to humans.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this argument is effective as a reductio ad absurdum. The Qadariyya must either deny their premise or accept this heretical consequence.
This shows the necessity of affirming divine decree (qadar) as the Ash'ari do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : إذا كان القاتل عندكم قادراً على أن لا يقتل هذا المقتول، فيعيش فهو قادر على قطع أجله وتقديمه قبل أجله، وهو قادر على تأخيره إلى أجله فالإنسان على قولكم يقدر أن يقدم آجال العباد ويؤخرها، ويقدر أن يبقي العباد ويبلغهم ويخرج أرواحهم، وهذا إلحاد في الدين
And we say to them: If the murderer, according to you, is able not to kill this murdered person, so he lives, then he is able to cut off his term and advance it before its time, and he is able to delay it to its time. Thus man, according to your statement, is able to advance and delay the terms of servants, and is able to keep servants alive, cause them to reach their destinies, and extract their souls, and this is heresy in religion.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum against the Qadariyya. The author builds on the previous argument: if the murderer truly has the ability not to kill, then he can spare the victim's life. From this it follows that the murderer can alter the appointed term (ajal): he can cut it short by killing, or delay it by not killing. This implies that humans have control over life and death, a prerogative belonging solely to Allah. Al-Ash'ari concludes that this belief constitutes heresy (ilhad fi al-din), as it attributes divine power to humans.

Key Terms
القدرة
الأجل
الإلحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Qadariyya deny divine decree entirely; actually they affirm it but limit it, leading to human control over terms.
Correction: The passage shows that even limited human control over actions logically leads to human control over appointed terms, which is heresy.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari label the Qadariyya's position on the murderer's power as 'heresy'?
A) Because it denies the existence of God.
B) Because it gives humans the ability to control the terms of life and death.
C) Because it denies the Qur'anic verses on terms.
D) Because it claims the murderer is not sinful.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari shows that the Qadariyya belief in human ability leads inevitably to attributing divine control over life and death to humans, which is heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if your choices control your lifespan, forgetting that Allah alone decrees the term? How does this lesson expose that subtle arrogance? Commit today to recite 'Say: Indeed, the death from which you flee will surely meet you' (Quran 62:8) whenever you feel overconfident in your planning.
?
When someone dies, do you ever harbor the thought, 'If only they had done this or that, they would still be alive'? According to al-Ash'ari, this thinking implies humans control the term. Repent from such thoughts and affirm that Allah's decree is just. Write down one area where you need to surrender your illusion of control over life and death.
?
The Qadariyya were accused of heresy for attributing divine power to humans. Where in your own heart do you subtly claim ownership over your life—your health, your achievements, your future? What practical step can you take today to return the control to Allah in your mind and actions?

The Provision of Forbidden Food
Ash'ari presents a dilemma about provision of unlawful food.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's argument that all provision, including that obtained unlawfully, is from Allah, and the logical consequences of denying this.

Core Idea
All sustenance is from Allah, even that which is unlawfully obtained.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari engages his opponents—likely the Mu'tazila or those who deny that Allah provides forbidden things—with a logical dilemma about provision (rizq). He presents a scenario of a person who eats only unlawfully usurped food and forces them to face the implications: either Allah provides that unlawful food, contradicting their doctrine, or they must accept that the person was not provided by Allah at all, leading to the absurdity of multiple providers. This lesson unpacks that argument and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is a typical kalam reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice how Ash'ari presents two possible answers to his question and then shows the unacceptable consequence of each.
If they say yes (Allah provided it), they abandon qadar. If they say no, they face the problem of multiple providers.
3
Analyze the consequence of denying provision
Focus on the second horn: if Allah did not provide the unlawful food, then the person's body grew from something not provided by Allah—this implies a provider other than Allah, which is shirk.
Ash'ari calls this 'great disbelief' (kufr 'azim).
4
Reflect on the overall theological point
Consider the broader lesson: rizq (provision) includes all that sustains the body, regardless of how it is acquired. Allah is the sole provider of all nourishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : خبرونا عمن اغتصب طعاماً فأكله حراماً، هل رزقه الله ذلك الحرام؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا القدر وإن قالوا: لا . قيل لهم : فمن أكل جميع عمره الحرام، فما رزقه الله شيئاً اغتذى به . ويقال لهم : فإذا كان غيره يغتصب له ذلك الطعام، ويطعمه إياه إلى أن مات فرازق هذا الإنسان عندكم غير الله وفي هذا إقرار منهم أن للخلق رازقين : أحدهما يرزق الحلال والآخر يرزق الحرام، وأن الناس تنبت لحومهم وتشد عظامهم، والله غير رازق لهم ما اغتذوا به . وإذا قلتم : إن الله لم يرزقه الحرام، لزمكم أن الله لم يغذه به، ولا جعله قواماً لجسمه، وأن لحمه وجسمه قام، وعظمه اشتد بغير الله عز وجل، وهو من رزقه الحرام، وهذا كفر عظيم إن احتملوا .
And it is said to them: Tell us about one who usurped food and ate it unlawfully—did Allah provide him that unlawful food? If they say: Yes, they abandon predestination (al-qadar). If they say: No. It is said to them: Then if one eats unlawful food his entire life, then Allah has not provided him anything by which he is nourished. And it is said to them: If someone else usurps that food for him and feeds it to him until he dies, then his provider according to you is other than Allah. And in this is an acknowledgment from them that creation has providers: one provides lawful and the other provides unlawful, and that people's flesh grows and their bones strengthen, while Allah is not the provider of what they are nourished by. And if you say that Allah did not provide him the unlawful, it necessarily follows that Allah did not nourish him by it, nor made it the support of his body, and that his flesh and body stood and his bone strengthened without Allah Almighty, while it is from His provision which is unlawful; and this is great disbelief (kufr) if they accept it.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an argument about provision (rizq). Ash'ari questions his opponents about a person who eats unlawfully usurped food: did Allah provide that unlawful food? If they say yes, then they abandon their doctrine of qadar (which denies Allah's involvement in evil). If they say no, then Ash'ari points out the consequence: if a person eats only unlawful food his whole life, then Allah provided him nothing nourishing. But then someone else must be providing it, implying that there are multiple providers: one for lawful and one for unlawful. This leads to the conclusion that bodies grow from provision not provided by Allah, which is absurd. Ash'ari argues that if Allah did not provide the unlawful food, then He did not cause the body to be nourished by it, and the body's growth would happen without Allah's power, which is kufr. The argument forces the opponent to either admit that Allah provides all rizq (including unlawful) or face the implications of multiple providers.

Key Terms
رزق (rizq: provision)
حرام (haram: unlawful)
كفر (kufr: disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah only provides halal (lawful) sustenance; haram is not from Allah.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that all sustenance, including haram, is from Allah because it nourishes the body. Denying this leads to the absurdity of multiple providers or kufr.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari say about a person who eats only unlawful food his entire life?
A) Allah did not provide him anything to sustain him.
B) Allah provided him only lawful food.
C) The person is not nourished by that food.
D) The food is not actually unlawful.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari demonstrates that all nourishment, whether halal or haram, is ultimately provided by Allah; to deny this undermines tawhid and leads to kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat your next meal, regardless of how it was obtained, can you genuinely say in your heart that it is from Allah alone? If not, what does that reveal about the depth of your tawhid?
?
Have you ever looked down on someone who earns through means you consider unlawful, thinking that their provision is not from Allah? What does Ash'ari's argument demand you to reconsider about your own attitude?
?
If you were to live your entire life on unlawful food, would you still believe that Allah is your Provider? What does your answer say about the strength of your reliance on Allah?

5 min
Refuting Ownership Condition for Divine Provision
Ash'ari refutes ownership condition using infant/animal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari refutes the claim that Allah cannot provide unlawful food by demonstrating that provision does not require ownership.

Core Idea
Allah can provide without owning—provision is not ownership.

In this passage, Ash'ari confronts a Qadari argument: if Allah provided unlawful food, He would own it, which is impossible. Ash'ari counters with everyday examples—a nursing infant and a grazing animal—both receive provision from Allah without owning what they receive. This undermines the objection and clarifies a key theological principle: provision does not imply ownership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure: question, answer, counter-question.
The passage is a dialectical refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and counterexample
Find the opponent's assertion: Allah cannot provide the unlawful because that would mean He owns it. Then identify Ash'ari's counterexample: the infant and the animal.
3
Understand the logical implication
Reflect on the conclusion: provision and ownership are separate. Allah provides things without owning them in a human sense. This refutes the opponent's objection.
This is a key distinction in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: لم أبيتم أن يرزق الله الحرام؟ فإن قالوا: لأنه لو رزق الحرام لملك الحرام. يقال لهم : خبرونا عن الطفل الذي يتغذى من لبن أمه، وعن البهيمة التي ترعى الحشيش، من يرزقهما ذلك؟ فإن قالوا : الله . قيل لهم : فمن ملكهما؟ وهل للبهيمة ملك؟ فإن قالوا: لا . قيل لهم : فلم زعمتم أنه لو رزق الحرام لملك الحرام، وقد يرزق الله الشيء ولا يُملكه؟
And it is said to them: Why did you refuse that Allah provides the unlawful? If they say: Because if He provided the unlawful, He would own the unlawful. It is said to them: Tell us about the infant who feeds from his mother's milk, and about the animal that grazes grass—who provides them that? If they say: Allah. It is said to them: Then who owns them? Does the animal own [anything]? If they say: No. It is said to them: Then why did you claim that if He provided the unlawful, He would own it, when Allah provides something without owning it?
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya argument that Allah cannot provide unlawful food because that would entail Him owning the unlawful (which is contradictory since unlawful things cannot be owned by Allah). Ash'ari uses a counterexample: an infant fed by his mother's milk and an animal grazing grass. These are provided by Allah, yet neither the infant nor the animal owns that provision. Ownership is not a necessary condition for provision. Therefore, the opponents' objection fails: Allah can provide unlawful food without owning it. The argument is analogical: just as Allah provides milk to the infant without the infant owning it, He can provide unlawful food without owning it.

Key Terms
حرام
ملك
رزق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah provides something, He must own it.
Correction: Allah can provide things without owning them in a human sense, as seen in the examples of infants and animals.

Checkpoint
How does Ash'ari refute the claim that Allah cannot provide unlawful food because He would then own it?
A) By arguing that Allah owns everything anyway.
B) By giving the example of infants and animals who are provided without ownership.
C) By stating that unlawful food is not really provision.
D) By denying that the food is unlawful.

Key Takeaway
Provision does not imply ownership; Allah's provision is not limited by human concepts of ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a blessing in your life as 'not from Allah' because it came through a means you considered unlawful or unfair? After this lesson, can you acknowledge that Allah provides without owning, and that His provision is not limited by your judgment? Commit to thanking Him for everything you receive, even the difficult gifts.
?
The opponents limited Allah's power based on their own logic. In what areas of your life do you find yourself imposing human limitations on Allah's sovereignty? How would your trust in Him grow if you truly believed He can provide goodness from any situation? Write a concrete decision to surrender that limitation today.
?
If provision and ownership are separate in Allah's hands, then what does that mean for your attachment to worldly possessions? Are you more attached to 'owning' things or to recognizing them as Allah's provision without claim? Let this lesson push you to hold loosely to what you have, knowing the Provider does not own in the way you do.

The Implication of Denying God Provides Haram
Al-Ash'ari argues that denying God's provision of haram leads to multiple providers and kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying that God provides haram sustenance leads to the absurd conclusion of multiple providers and kufr, according to Al-Ash'ari's logical argument.

Core Idea
Denying God's provision of haram requires belief in multiple providers, which is shirk.

Al-Ash'ari presents a sharp logical argument against those who claim that God only provides halal sustenance. He forces them to confront the implication: if God does not provide haram, then who does? And if another being provides it, then there are multiple providers, a form of shirk. This passage demonstrates how kalam reasoning exposes hidden consequences of theological positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional opening
Notice the two positions: if they say they abandoned qadr (affirming God's decree includes haram) or if they deny that, then ask about the person who eats only haram all his life.
The argument begins with a conditional that sets up the dilemma.
2
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the scenario: if someone else usurps food and feeds the person, then that usurper becomes the provider, leading to multiple providers (one for halal, one for haram).
This exposes the absurdity of denying God's provision.
3
Grasp the final implication
If they insist God did not provide haram, then they must say God did not nourish that person's body, which means the body grew without God's provision – a kufr.
The argument concludes that this position necessitates disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا القدر وإن قالوا: لا . قيل لهم : فمن أكل جميع عمره الحرام، فما رزقه الله شيئاً اغتذى به ويقال لهم : فإذا كان غيره يغتصب له ذلك الطعام، ويطعمه إياه إلى أن مات فرازق هذا الإنسان عندكم غير الله وفي هذا إقرار منهم أن للخلق رازقين : أحدهما يرزق الحلال والآخر يرزق الحرام، وأن الناس تنبت لحومهم وتشد عظامهم، والله غير رازق لهم ما اغتذوا به . وإذا قلتم : إن الله لم يرزقه الحرام، لزمكم أن الله لم يغذه به، ولا جعله قواماً لجسمه، وأن لحمه وجسمه قام، وعظمه اشتد بغير الله عز وجل، وهو من رزقه الحرام، وهذا كفر عظيم إن احتملوا .
If they say: Yes, they have abandoned qadr. And if they say: No, it is said to them: Whoever eats haram all his life, then God did not provide him anything he nourished himself with. And it is said to them: If someone else usurps that food for him and feeds it to him until he dies, then the provider of this human, according to you, is other than God. And in this is an acknowledgment from them that the creation has two providers: one provides halal and the other provides haram, and that people's flesh grows and their bones become strong, while God is not providing them what they nourished themselves with. And if you say: God did not provide him the haram, then it follows that God did not nourish him with it, nor made it the support of his body, and that his flesh and body stood and his bone became strong by other than God Almighty, and he is from His provision of haram, and this is great kufr if they tolerate it.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against those who deny that God provides haram sustenance. Al-Ash'ari begins by posing a conditional: If they say that God provides only halal, then they have abandoned qadr (divine decree). If they deny this, then ask them: What about a person who eats only haram all his life? Does that mean God did not provide him anything? Then he argues that if someone else usurps food and feeds it to him, then according to them, that usurper is the provider, not God. This leads to acknowledging two providers: one for halal and one for haram. Furthermore, if they insist God does not provide haram, then they must say God did not nourish that person's body, which means his body grew by something other than God's provision, which is kufr.

Key Terms
رزق (rizq: provision)
حرام (haram: forbidden)
قدر (qadr: divine decree)
كفر (kufr: disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God providing haram implies He approves of sin.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that God's provision of haram is a creative act, not an approval; the sin lies in the consumption, not the provision.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari, what follows from denying that God provides haram sustenance?
A) It implies there are multiple providers.
B) It implies God is unjust.
C) It implies human free will.
D) It implies God does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's provision of haram leads to the heretical belief in multiple providers and undermines tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every morsel of food you eat—whether earned lawfully or not—comes from God alone? Examine your heart: is there any area of your life where you subtly attribute your sustenance to your own efforts or to someone else, rather than to God's provision? Reflect on your recent meals and ask yourself: would I be comfortable saying 'God provided this' even if it came from a questionable source?
?
If you were faced with a situation where only haram food was available and you were starving, would your theology allow you to say 'God is providing this' while still rejecting the sin? How does al-Ash'ari's argument challenge you to reconcile God's absolute sovereignty with your moral responsibility? Can you accept that God may test you with provision through means you dislike, without attributing evil to Him?
?

If you have ever consumed anything haram, did you consider that your very physical existence depended on that provision from God? How does this truth move you to repent and rely solely on His halal provision, trusting that He alone sustains?

The Logic of Divine Guidance and Abandonment
Al-Ash'ari argues that khidhlan is the creation of kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that God's abandonment of disbelievers is the creation of disbelief within them, and why alternative definitions lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Divine abandonment is not mere withdrawal but the creation of disbelief.

Al-Ash'ari presents a tight logical argument to establish that God's abandonment (khidhlan) of disbelievers is equivalent to His creating disbelief in them. He forces the opponent to choose between affirming this or facing a contradiction that leads to an absurd conclusion—that God would have abandoned believers as well. This passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning: taking the opponent's own premises and driving them to their necessary conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the translation. Note the structure: it begins with a conditional question and proceeds through several syllogisms.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'قيل لهم' — it marks each step of the argument.
2
Identify the main terms
Identify the key terms: tawfiq, khidhlan, kufr, takhliyah. Understand how they are used in the argument.
Tawfiq is 'guidance/success' from God; khidhlan is 'abandonment'; takhliyah is 'leaving alone'.
3
Follow the logical progression
Trace the argument: (1) If tawfiq is from God, then khidhlan must also be from God. (2) If you deny that khidhlan is from God, you must say God granted success to disbelievers—but then they would be protected from kufr, which contradicts their actual kufr. (3) If you affirm khidhlan from God, then what is it? Either it is the creation of kufr or mere 'leaving alone'. (4) If 'leaving alone', then God also left believers alone with kufr, so He would have abandoned them too—an absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's own view leads to a contradiction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The only consistent position is that khidhlan is the creation of kufr in the disbeliever. This affirms God's absolute sovereignty over all actions, including disbelief.
This directly refutes the Qadariyya who denied that God creates human acts.
5
Connect to the broader doctrine of qadr
Reflect on how this argument supports the Ash'ari position that all actions—good and evil—are created by God, while humans acquire them.
This is a key proof in the qadr debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : إذا كان توفيق المؤمنين بالله، فما أنكرتم أن يكون خذلان الكافرين من قبل الله تعالى وإلا فإن زعمتم أن الله وفق الكافرين للإيمان فقولوا : عصمهم من الكفر ، وكيف يعصمهم من الكفر وقد وقع الكفر منهم ؟ فإن أثبتوا أن الله خذلهم ، قيل لهم : فالخذلان من الله أليس هو الكفر الذي خلقه فيهم؟ فإن قالوا: نعم وافقوا . وإن قالوا: لا قيل لهم : فما ذلك الخذلان الذي خلقه؟ فإن قالوا : تَخْلِيّته إياهم والكفر . قيل لهم أو ليس من قولكم إن الله عز وجل خلى بين المؤمنين وبين الكفر فمن قولهم : نعم . قيل لهم : فإذا كان الخذلان التخلية بينهم وبين الكفر، فقد لزمكم أن يكون خذل المؤمنين؛ لأنه خلى بينهم وبين الكفر، وهذا خروج عن الدين، فلا بد لهم أن يثبتوا لهم الخذلان للكفر الذي خلقه فيهم، فيتركوا القول بالقدر .
It is said to them: If the success (tawfiq) of the believers is from God, then why do you deny that the abandonment (khidhlan) of the disbelievers is from God Almighty? Otherwise, if you claim that God granted success (waffaqa) to the disbelievers for faith, then say: He protected them from kufr. But how could He protect them from kufr while kufr occurred from them? If they affirm that God abandoned (khadhala) them, it is said to them: Is not the abandonment from God the kufr that He created in them? If they say: Yes, they agree. And if they say: No, it is said to them: Then what is that abandonment that He created? If they say: His leaving them alone (takhliyah) with kufr. It is said to them: Is it not from your statement that God Almighty left (khalla) between the believers and kufr? So they say: Yes. It is said to them: So if the abandonment is the leaving alone between them and kufr, then it necessarily follows that He abandoned the believers, because He left between them and kufr. And this is apostasy from the religion. So they must affirm for them the abandonment for the kufr that He created in them, so that they may abandon the doctrine of qadr.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against those who deny that God creates kufr. Al-Ash'ari argues that if believers' guidance (tawfiq) is from God, then the abandonment (khidhlan) of disbelievers must also be from God. If they claim God granted success to disbelievers, then they must say He protected them from kufr, but kufr occurred. If they say God abandoned them, then the abandonment is either the creation of kufr or merely leaving them alone. If it is leaving alone, then God also left believers alone with kufr (since He did not force them), which would mean He abandoned them too, leading to apostasy. Therefore, they must admit that God creates kufr in them.

Key Terms
توفيق (tawfiq: divine guidance)
خذلان (khidhlan: abandonment)
الكفر (kufr: disbelief)
تخلية (takhliyah: leaving alone)
قدر (qadr: divine decree)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think khidhlan is merely God withholding grace without creating anything.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that if khidhlan were merely 'leaving alone', it would necessarily apply to believers as well, which is false. Therefore, khidhlan must be an act of creation—specifically, the creation of kufr in the disbeliever.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if khidhlan is defined as 'leaving alone with kufr'?
A) It means God abandoned believers too.
B) It means God is merciful.
C) It means humans have free will.
D) It means God does not create acts.

Key Takeaway
God's abandonment of disbelievers is the creation of disbelief in them, affirming His absolute sovereignty over all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God creates both faith and disbelief, do you truly surrender your own sense of autonomy when you see people rejecting Him? Or do you secretly resent that He does not 'force' them to believe?
?
When you witness someone persist in sin or disbelief, do your thoughts about their accountability align with affirming that God's will is supreme, or do you find yourself judging as if they act independently of Him?
?
This teaching forces you to choose: will you accept that every act, including your own obedience or rebellion, is created by God? If so, does that humbly drive you to rely on His tawfiq, or does it tempt you to despair of your own efforts?

Khidhlan: Divine Abandonment as Creation of Disbelief
Ash'ari presents a dialectical argument to prove that khidhlan is the creation of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari argues that khidhlan (divine abandonment) is the creation of disbelief in the disbeliever, refuting the Qadariyya position.

Core Idea
Khidhlan is not mere abandonment—it is Allah's creation of kufr.

This lesson presents a dialectical argument from Imam al-Ash'ari's Al-Ibanah. He challenges the Qadariyya who deny that Allah creates human actions. By analyzing the concepts of tawfiq (divine success) and khidhlan (abandonment), Ash'ari demonstrates that the only consistent position is that Allah creates disbelief in the disbeliever. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the dialogue style: questions and answers.
2
Understand the initial question
Ash'ari asks whether God empowers the slave over the forbidden without giving ownership. This sets up the issue of divine causation.
The opponent's answer leads to a contradiction.
3
Trace the tawfiq–khidhlan parallel
If tawfiq (success) is from God for believers, then khidhlan (abandonment) must also be from God for disbelievers. Otherwise, consistency fails.
The opponent cannot claim God grants success but not abandonment.
4
Examine the two options for khidhlan
Khidhlan is either the creation of disbelief or mere leaving alone. If the latter, God abandons believers too, which is absurd.
Thus, khidhlan must be the creation of kufr.
5
Conclude the argument
The only way to avoid absurdity is to affirm that khidhlan is the creation of disbelief, which necessitates accepting predestination (qadar).
This refutes the Qadariyya denial of divine creation of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : هل أقدر الله العبد على الحرام ولم يُملكه إياه؟
فإن قالوا: نعم. يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يرزقه الحرام، وإن لم يُملكه
٢٣٣- جواب :
يقال لهم : إذا كان توفيق المؤمنين بالله، فما أنكرتم أن يكون خذلان الكافرين من قبل الله تعالى وإلا فإن زعمتم أن الله وفق الكافرين للإيمان فقولوا : عصمهم من الكفر ، وكيف يعصمهم من الكفر وقد وقع الكفر منهم ؟ فإن أثبتوا أن الله خذلهم ، قيل لهم : فالخذلان من الله أليس هو
الكفر ؟
فإن قالوا: نعم وافقوا .
وإن قالوا: لا
•
قيل لهم : فما ذلك الخذلان الذي خلقه؟
فإن قالوا : تَخْلِيته إياهم والكفر .
قيل لهم أو ليس من قولكم إن الله عز وجل خلى بين المؤمنين وبين
فمن قولهم : نعم .
قيل لهم : فإذا كان الخذلان التخلية بينهم وبين الكفر، فقد لزمكم أن يكون خذل المؤمنين؛ لأنه خلى بينهم وبين الكفر، وهذا خروج عن الدين، فلا بد لهم أن يثبتوا لهم الخذلان للكفر الذي خلقه فيهم، فيتركوا القول بالقدر .
And it is said to them: Did God empower the slave over the forbidden but not give him ownership of it? If they say: Yes. It is said to them: Then what prevents you from [saying] that He provides him with the forbidden, even though He did not give him ownership? 233 - Answer: It is said to them: If the granting of success (tawfiq) to the believers is from God, then what prevents you from [saying] that the abandonment (khidhlan) of the disbelievers is from God, may He be exalted? Otherwise, if you claim that God granted success to the disbelievers for faith, then say: He protected them from disbelief. But how can He protect them from disbelief when disbelief has occurred from them? If they affirm that God abandoned them, it is said to them: Is not abandonment from God the same as disbelief? If they say: Yes, they agree. If they say: No, it is said to them: Then what is that abandonment that He created? If they say: His leaving them alone with disbelief. It is said to them: Is it not from your statement that God, might and majesty, left the believers alone with...? They say: Yes. It is said to them: If abandonment is leaving them alone with disbelief, then it necessarily follows that He has abandoned the believers, because He left them alone with disbelief, and this is a departure from the religion. So they must affirm for them abandonment for the disbelief that He created in them, so that they abandon the doctrine of qadar.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) who deny that God creates the actions of slaves. The author, Imam al-Ash'ari, poses a series of questions. First, he asks whether God empowers the slave over the forbidden (haram) without giving him ownership (tamleek) of it. If they answer yes, he points out the inconsistency: why then can God not provide the forbidden? Then he moves to the concept of tawfiq (divine granting of success) for believers and argues that if tawfiq is from God, then khidhlan (abandonment, withholding success) for disbelievers must also be from God. He forces the opponent to admit that khidhlan is either the creation of kufr or mere leaving alone. If they say it is creation of kufr, they agree. If they say it is leaving alone, then the same would apply to believers (since God leaves them alone with the possibility of disbelief), which is absurd. Thus the only consistent position is that khidhlan is the creation of kufr in them, and therefore they must accept predestination.

Key Terms
توفيق (tawfiq)
خذلان (khidhlan)
قدر (qadar)
خلق (khalq)
تمليك (tamleek)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think khidhlan is merely God leaving the slave alone without interference.
Correction: Imam al-Ash'ari argues that if khidhlan were mere leaving alone, then God would also be abandoning believers (since He leaves them alone with the possibility of disbelief) — a clear absurdity. Therefore, khidhlan must be the active creation of disbelief in the disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the Qadariyya opponent admit if they say khidhlan is merely leaving the slave alone with disbelief?
A) That God abandons believers as well.
B) That God creates disbelief in the slave.
C) That the slave creates his own disbelief.
D) That khidhlan is impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
Khidhlan is not mere passivity; it is Allah's active creation of disbelief in the disbeliever, consistent with His attribute of creating all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that even your faith (tawfiq) is a direct gift from Allah, or do you secretly credit your own efforts? If His tawfiq were withdrawn, would you still have faith?
?
When you see a disbeliever, do you recognize that their disbelief is created by Allah as an act of khidhlan? How does this shape your humility and gratitude for your own iman?
?
If khidhlan is not merely Allah leaving someone alone but His active creation of disbelief, what does that tell you about the seriousness of sin and the need to constantly seek Allah's protection and tawfiq? Are you living with that urgency?

The Two Types of Afflictions: Patience vs. Abandonment
Ash'ari distinguishes two types of afflictions requiring patience or abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a Muslim is always in a state of either blessing (ni'mah) or affliction (baliyyah), and that afflictions are of two types: those requiring patience and those requiring active abandonment of sin.

Core Idea
Not all afflictions require patience; sins demand abandonment.

In this passage from Al-Ibanah, Imam Ash'ari responds to a question from a Qadari (a proponent of free will) about the human condition. He establishes that every person is perpetually in either a state of blessing or affliction, and that the response to affliction depends on its nature. This distinction is critical for understanding Islamic ethics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a question and a detailed answer.
2
Identify the three categories presented
The passage mentions blessing (requiring gratitude), afflictions requiring patience, and afflictions requiring abandonment. List them in your mind.
3
Distinguish between the two types of afflictions
Note that not all afflictions are the same; some must be patiently endured (like illness), others must be actively abandoned (like sin).
This is the key point of the lesson.
4
Apply the lesson to your own life
Consider examples of afflictions you face and classify them into those requiring patience and those requiring abandonment.
This helps internalize the teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٣٤ - مسألة :
إن سأل سائل من أهل القدر، فقال: هل يخلو العبد من أن يكون بين
يجب عليه أن يشكر الله عليها ، أو بلية يجب عليه الصبر عليها ؟ قيل له : العبد لا يخلو من نعمة ،وبلية والنعمة يجب على العبد أن يشكر الله عليها والبلايا على ضربين :
منها ما يجب الصبر عليها كالأمراض والأسقام وما أشبه ذلك
ومنها ما يجب عليه الإقلاع عنها كالكفر والمعاصي .
234 - Question: If a questioner from the people of qadar asks: Is the slave ever free from being either in a blessing for which he must thank God, or an affliction for which he must be patient? It is said to him: The slave is never free from a blessing and an affliction. The blessing requires the slave to thank God, while afflictions are of two types: among them are those for which patience is required, such as diseases, illnesses, and the like; and among them are those for which he must abandon them, such as disbelief and sins.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a brief classification. The Qadari (proponent of free will) asks whether a person is ever free from being in a state of blessing or affliction. The answer is no: everyone is always in one of these two states. The ruling for a blessing is gratitude (shukr). For afflictions, there are two categories: (1) those requiring patience (sabr), like illnesses; (2) those requiring abandonment (iqla') and cessation, like disbelief and sins. This classification is important because it shows that not all afflictions are to be endured; some must be actively abandoned. The passage does not explicitly tie this to divine predestination, but it sets up a framework for discussing responsibility.

Key Terms
نعمة (ni'mah)
بلية (baliyyah)
صبر (sabr)
إقلاع (iqla')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All afflictions require patience.
Correction: Some afflictions, like sins, require abandonment, not patience.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how many types of afflictions (balaya) are there?
A) One type: patience is required.
B) Two types: those requiring patience and those requiring abandonment.
C) Three types: patience, gratitude, and abandonment.
D) Only afflictions of the body require patience.

Key Takeaway
Afflictions are of two kinds: those to be patiently endured and those to be actively abandoned, such as sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you tend to passively endure it as if it were a trial you cannot change, or do you actively abandon it? What would it take for you to treat your sins as afflictions that require immediate abandonment, not patience?
?

?
The passage says you are never free from a blessing and an affliction. In this very moment, what blessing and what affliction are you experiencing? How should you respond to each based on this teaching?

He quotes the opening of Surah al-Baqarah, where Allah states that the Quran is guidance for the pious. On Blessings and Trials and the Servant's Duty
Al-Ash'ari answers an objection from the Qadariyya about the servant's duty regarding blessings and trials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the servant is never free from either a blessing (requiring gratitude) or a trial, and that trials are of two types requiring different responses: patience or abandonment.

Core Idea
The servant is always either under a blessing or a trial — each has its own proper response.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a question from the Qadariyya, who argued about human free will. He clarifies that the servant is never without obligation: either thankfulness for blessings or patient endurance (or abandonment) for trials. This reframes everyday states as theological duties.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the question from the Qadariyya: Is the servant ever free from being in a blessing or a trial?
The Qadariyya denied predestination and emphasized free will.
2
Note the universal state
Al-Ash'ari answers definitively: the servant is never free from either a blessing or a trial. This establishes that every moment involves theological duty.
3
Distinguish two types of trials
Trials are divided into two categories: those requiring patience (like illness) and those requiring abandonment (like sin). Recognize the different obligations.
Patience is for unavoidable trials; abandonment is for avoidable sins.
4
Connect to broader aqida
This classification refutes the idea that all hardship is simply to be endured; some must be actively stopped. It also shows the servant is always accountable.
This passage reinforces that Allah's decree includes both blessings and trials, and the servant's duty is clear in each case.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. Al-Ash'ari anticipates an objection from the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) who deny predestination and insist on complete human free will. The answer is no: the servant is always in one of these two states. Al-Ash'ari then clarifies the two types of trials: some require patience (e.g., illness) — these are afflictions beyond one's control where patience is the duty; others require abandonment (al-iqlaa') — these are sins and disbelief, which the servant must actively cease. This distinction refutes the notion that all trials are alike or that the servant is ever without obligation due to external circumstances.

An Argument by Analogy: Allah's Guidance and Iblis's Misguidance
Al-Ash'ari draws an analogy between divine guidance and satanic misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses a logical analogy between Allah's call to faith and Iblis's call to disbelief to argue that guidance and misguidance depend on the recipient's acceptance.

Core Idea
If Iblis's call only misguides those who accept it, then Allah's call only guides those who accept it.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari presents a powerful rhetorical question aimed at the Mu'tazila. He draws a parallel between divine guidance and satanic misguidance, arguing that the effect of a call depends on the response of the listener. This argument challenges the Mu'tazilite notion that Allah's call to faith is inherently guiding for all, and instead shows that guidance is contingent upon acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Ash'ari uses a very concise, rhetorical style.
2
Identify the two calls being compared
The passage compares Allah's call to faith (دعاء الله إلى الإيمان) and Iblis's call to disbelief (دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر). Note how each is described as either guidance (هدى) or misguidance (إضلال).
3
Understand the logical structure of the analogy
Al-Ash'ari first assumes the Mu'tazila's position that Allah's call is guidance for all. Then he asks why they do not apply the same logic to Iblis's call. When they would likely object, he turns the argument: if effect depends on acceptance, then the same applies to Allah's call.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Consider the implication for the doctrine of guidance
The analogy implies that Allah's guidance is not universal; it is only effective for those who accept it. This counters the Mu'tazilite view that Allah must guide all people equally.
5
Reflect on al-Ash'ari's rhetorical style
Notice how al-Ash'ari uses a series of questions to force the opponent into a contradiction. His style is dialectical and persuasive.
This is typical of early kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٣٧- مسألة أخرى : ثم يقال لهم : إذا جاز أن يكون دعاء الله إلى الإيمان هدى لمن قبل ولمن لم يقبل ، فما أنكرتم دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر إضلالاً لمن قبل ولمن لم يقبل، فإن كان دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر إضلالاً للكافرين الذين قبلوا عنه، دون المؤمنين الذين لم يقبلوا عنه، فما أنكرتم أن دعاء الله عز وجل إلى الإيمان هدى للمؤمنين الذين قبلوا عنه، دون الكافرين الذين لم يقبلوا عنه، وإلا فما الفرق بين ذلك؟
237 - Another issue: Then it is said to them: If it is possible that Allah's call to faith is guidance for those who accept and those who do not accept, then why do you deny that Iblis's call to disbelief is misguidance for those who accept and those who do not accept? If Iblis's call to disbelief is misguidance for the disbelievers who accepted it, not for the believers who did not accept it, then why do you deny that Allah's call to faith is guidance for the believers who accepted it, not for the disbelievers who did not accept it? Otherwise, what is the difference between that?
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an argument by analogy (qiyas). Al-Ash'ari first posits that the Mu'tazila claim that Allah's call to faith (da'wa) is guidance for both those who accept it and those who reject it. He then asks: if that is the case, then by the same logic, Iblis's call to disbelief should be considered misguidance (idlal) for both those who accept it and those who reject it. But the Mu'tazila would likely object: Iblis's call only misguides those who accept it, not those who reject it. Al-Ash'ari then turns the argument: if the effect depends on acceptance, then similarly, Allah's call to faith only guides those who accept it (the believers), not those who reject it (the disbelievers). Thus, the Mu'tazila are forced to admit that Allah's guidance is not universal, contrary to their doctrine. He ends by challenging them to explain the difference between the two cases if they disagree.

Key Terms
دعاء
إضلال
هدى
إيمان
كفر
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's call to faith is inherently effective on all people irrespective of their response.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the effect depends on acceptance; just as Iblis's call only misguides those who accept, Allah's call only guides those who accept.

Checkpoint

B) Allah's call versus Iblis's call
C) The Quran versus previous scriptures

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari uses an analogy between Allah's call and Iblis's call to demonstrate that guidance and misguidance are contingent upon the recipient's acceptance, not merely the caller's action.

Al-Ash'ari's argument strips away excuses. If you find yourself less guided than you should be, is it because Allah's call failed, or because you have not wholeheartedly accepted it? What will you do this week to ensure your acceptance is genuine?

Analogy between God's call to faith and Iblis's call to disbelief
Al-Ash'ari poses an analogy to refute the claim that God's call guides all equally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses an analogy between God's call and Iblis's call to refute the claim that divine guidance is universal in effect.

Core Idea
God's call guides only those who accept it, just as Iblis's call misguides only those who accept it.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari presents a powerful logical analogy to challenge the Mu'tazilite claim that God's call to faith is guidance for everyone equally. He points out that if Iblis's call to disbelief only misguides those who accept it, then consistency requires that God's call to faith only guides those who accept it. This argument forces the opponent to either accept the conclusion or provide a distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of the argument sink in.
Notice the structure: 'then it is said to them' introduces the objection.
2
Identify the two calls being compared
Al-Ash'ari compares God's call to faith (دعاء الله إلى الإيمان) with Iblis's call to disbelief (دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر). Note how each is described as guidance or misguidance.
3
Trace the logical condition
The argument hinges on a conditional: 'if Iblis's call misguides only those who accept it, then why don't you admit that God's call guides only those who accept it?' Follow the 'if-then' structure.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the rhetorical force
The passage ends with 'What is the difference?' This demands a principled distinction from the opponent. Reflect on why no such distinction exists within the opponent's framework.
The common misunderstanding is that this is about justice; it's actually about logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذا جاز أن يكون دعاء الله إلى الإيمان هدى لمن قبل ولمن لم يقبل ، فما أنكرتم دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر إضلالاً لمن قبل ولمن لم يقبل، فإن كان دعاء إبليس إلى الكفر إضلالاً للكافرين الذين قبلوا عنه، دون المؤمنين الذين لم يقبلوا عنه، فما أنكرتم أن دعاء الله عز وجل إلى الإيمان هدى للمؤمنين الذين قبلوا عنه، دون الكافرين الذين لم يقبلوا عنه، وإلا فما الفرق بين ذلك؟
Then it is said to them: If it is permissible that God's call to faith is guidance for those who accept and those who do not accept, then why do you deny that Iblis's call to disbelief is misguidance for those who accept and those who do not accept? For if Iblis's call to disbelief is misguidance for the disbelievers who accepted from him, without the believers who did not accept from him, then why do you deny that God's call to faith is guidance for the believers who accepted from Him, without the disbelievers who did not accept from Him? Otherwise, what is the difference between that?
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument (مسألة) where al-Ash'ari poses a dilemma to his opponents, likely the Mu'tazila. The argument proceeds by analogy. First, he states the opponents' claim: God's call (دعاء) to faith is guidance (هدى) for both those who accept and those who reject it. Then he uses a parallel case: Iblis's call to disbelief. If the opponents accept that Iblis's call misguides only those who accept it (the disbelievers) and not those who reject it (the believers), then consistency demands the same for God's call: it guides only those who accept it (the believers), not those who reject it (the disbelievers). The rhetorical question 'what is the difference?' forces the opponents to either accept the analogy or provide a principled distinction, which they cannot.

هدى
إضلال
إبليس
كافر
مؤمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari is arguing about God's justice in guiding or misguiding people.
Correction: Actually, the argument is about logical consistency: if the opponent accepts that Iblis's call only misguides those who accept it, then they must also accept that God's call only guides those who accept it, unless they can provide a valid distinction.
